What Is Mantiq?

Defining the Simple Word (Mufrad) by Exclusion: The Four Types
Author defines mufrad by listing four types excluded from compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four exclusion categories a given word falls into, and determine whether it is simple (mufrad) or compound (murakkab).

Core Idea
A word is simple if its parts do not indicate parts of its intended meaning.

In logic, words are classified into simple (mufrad) and compound (murakkab). This passage from Qutbi's commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya defines mufrad by listing four types of words that are excluded from being compound. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing terms in definitions and syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basic criterion
A compound word has a part that indicates a part of its meaning. Simple is the negation: a word that does not have a part indicating a part of its intended meaning. Note that the 'intended meaning' is key—what the speaker intends to convey.
The criterion is about intentional indication, not mere semantic composition.
2
Examine the first two types
First type: words with no part at all (e.g., the interrogative hamza, a single letter). Second type: words with parts but those parts carry no meaning (e.g., 'Zayd' as a proper name; the letters zay, ya, dal individually mean nothing). Both fail the compound criterion because they have no part indicating a meaning.
3
Examine the third type
Words with a part that indicates a meaning, but that meaning is not part of the intended meaning of the whole. Example: 'Abdullah' used as a proper name. The part 'Abd' means 'servant', but the intended meaning is the specific person, not servitude. Servitude is an attribute outside the essence of the person.
The distinction between literal part meaning and intended whole meaning is crucial.
4
Examine the fourth type
Words with a part that indicates a part of the intended meaning, but that indication is not intended. Example: 'al-hayawan al-natiq' (the rational animal) used as a proper name for a human. The parts 'animal' and 'rational' do indicate parts of the human essence (the intended meaning conceptually), but when used as a proper name, the speaker intends only the particular person, not the conceptual composition. The indication is not the goal.
This is the most nuanced case: the parts semantically correspond to the meaning, but the speaker's intention bypasses the composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الألفاظ
فيخرج عن الحد مالا يكون له جزء أصلاً - كهمزة الاستفهام.
ومايكون له جزء لكن لادلالة له على معنى - كزيد.
وما يكون له جزء دالّ على المعنى، لكن ذلك المعنى لا يكون جزء المعنى المقصود - كـ «عبد الله » علماً، فإنّ له جزء كـ«عبد» دالاً على معنى، وهو العبودية، لكنه ليس جزء المعنى المقصود، أي الذات المشخصة).
وما يكون له جزء دال على جزء المعنى المقصود ولكن لا يكون دلالته مقصودة كـ «الحيوان الناطق» إذا سمي به شخص إنساني، فإن معناه حينئذ الماهية الإنسانية مع التشخص، و الماهية الإنسانية مجموع مفهومي الحيوان والناطق. فالحيوان مثلا - الذي هو جزء اللفظ - دال على جزء المعنى المقصود الذي هو الشخص الإنساني لأنه دال على مفهوم الحيوان )، لكن

كوم الرسالة الشمسية
والاً - أي وإن لم يقصد بجزء منه الدلالة على جزء معناه – فهو المفرد، سواء لم يكن له جزء أو كان له جزء ولم يدلّ على معنى، أو كان له جزء دال على معنى ولا يكون ذلك المعنى جزء المعنى المقصود من اللفظ كـ «عبد الله » ، أو كان له جزء دال على جزء المعنى المقصود لكن لم يكن دلالته مقصودة ; فحدّ المفرد يتناول الألفاظ الأربعة.
Topics of Words. There exits from the definition (of compound) what has no part at all - like the interrogative hamza. And what has a part but no indication of a meaning - like Zayd. And what has a part indicating a meaning, but that meaning is not part of the intended meaning - like 'Abdullah as a proper name, for it has a part like 'Abd' indicating a meaning, which is servitude, but it is not part of the intended meaning, i.e., the particular essence). And what has a part indicating a part of the intended meaning but its indication is not intended - like 'al-hayawan al-natiq' if it is used as a name for a human individual, for its meaning then is the human essence with individuation, and the human essence is the sum of the concepts of animal and rational. So 'animal' for example - which is part of the utterance - indicates a part of the intended meaning which is the human person because it indicates the concept of animal), but its concept is part of the human essence, and it is part of the meaning of the intended utterance; the indication of 'animal' on its concept is not intended in the state of being a proper name, rather the intended from 'al-hayawan al-natiq' is only the particular essence. (1) That is because servitude is an attribute of the particular essence and is not included in it, rather outside it, and similarly the word 'Allah' indicates a meaning but that meaning is also not a part of the particular essence, which is apparent. And he only said 'like Abdullah as a proper name' because if it were not a proper name, it would be an additive compound, like 'thrower of stones'. And likewise 'al-hayawan al-natiq' if it were not a proper name, it would be a restrictive compound of the described and the attribute (Sharif). (2) That is, the human essence is part of the intended meaning, so the concept of animal is also part of that intended meaning, because the part of a part is a part (Sharif). The Shamsiyya Treatise Book. The first - i.e., if a part of it is not intended to indicate a part of its meaning - then it is the simple (mufrad), whether it has no part, or it has a part and it does not indicate a meaning, or it has a part indicating a meaning and that meaning is not part of the intended meaning of the utterance like 'Abdullah', or it has a part indicating a part of the intended meaning but its indication is not intended; so the definition of the simple encompasses the four types.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the simple word (mufrad) by exclusion. The author begins a section on topics of words (Mabāhith al-Alfāẓ) and describes what is excluded from the definition of a compound. A compound is a word having a part that indicates a part of its meaning. Anything that does not meet this condition is simple. The author lists four cases that are excluded: (1) a word with no part at all, like the interrogative hamza (which is a single letter); (2) a word with parts but those parts do not indicate any meaning, like the proper name Zayd (its letters 'zay', 'ya', 'dal' individually carry no meaning); (3) a word with a part that indicates a meaning, but that meaning is not part of the intended meaning of the whole word, like 'Abdullah' when used as a proper name; the part 'Abd' indicates servitude, but the intended meaning is the particular person, and servitude is an attribute external to that essence; (4) a word with a part that indicates a part of the intended meaning, but that indication is not intended when the word is used as a proper name, like 'al-hayawan al-natiq' when naming a human individual; the literal parts indicate animality and rationality, which together constitute the human essence, but the intention is solely the particular person, not the conceptual composition. The footnote clarifies the distinction: when 'Abdullah' is not a proper name, it is an additive compound (like 'ramī al-ḥijāra', thrower of stones); similarly, 'al-ḥayawān al-nāṭiq' when not a proper name is a restrictive compound (adjective-noun phrase). The definition concludes: any word that falls into one of these four categories is simple (mufrad). This is an important logical classification because the distinction between simple and compound is foundational for analyzing terms in syllogisms and definitions.

مفرد
مركب
جزء
دلالة

D) 'Rami al-hijara' (thrower of stones) as a descriptive compound

Key Takeaway
The definition of simple (mufrad) is by exclusion: any word that does not have a part indicating a part of its intended meaning is simple, covering four distinct cases.

Why the Simple Word is Defined After the Compound
Author responds to an objection about ordering of mufrad and murakkab in definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the distinction between essence (dhat) and concept (mafhum) and explain why the simple is defined after the compound in logical definitions.

Core Idea
Definition follows conceptual order, not natural order — simples are conceptually posterior.

In this passage, the author anticipates an objection: simple words are naturally prior to compound words, so why define the compound first? The response reveals a key distinction between essence (what a word refers to in reality) and concept (the mental meaning). Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how definitions are ordered in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The opponent claims that the simple (mufrad) is naturally prior to the compound (murakkab), so defining the compound first violates natural order and is erroneous.
2
Distinguish the two aspects
The author introduces two considerations: essence (dhat) – the external individuals – and concept (mafhum) – the mental meaning. For example, 'writer' has an essence (Zayd, Amr) and a concept (a thing having writing).
3
Apply the distinction to natural priority
If the opponent means essence-priority, it is conceded but irrelevant to definitions. If they mean concept-priority, it is denied.
4
Understand the reasoning for concept-priority
The concept of the compound includes positive restrictions (existence), while the concept of the simple is privative (non-existence). Existence in conceptualization is prior to non-existence, so the compound's concept is conceptually prior.
5
Connect to the order in the book
Therefore, in definitions (which concern concepts), the compound comes first; in divisions and rulings (which concern essences), the simple comes first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : المفرد مقدم على المركب طبعاً، فلم أخره وضعاً، ومخالفة الوضع الطبع في قوة الخطإ عند المحصلين.
فنقول : للمفرد والمركب اعتباران : أحدهما بحسب الذات، وهو ماصدق عليه المفرد من زيد وعمرو ،وغيرهما وثانيهما بحسب المفهوم وهو ما وضع اللفظ بإزائه كالكاتب - مثلاً - فإن له مفهوماً هو شيء له الكتابة، وذاتاً هو ماصدق عليه الكاتب من أفراد الإنسان.
فإن عنيتم بقولكم : «المفرد مقدّم على المركب طبعاً» أن ذات المفرد مقدم على ذات المركب، فمسلّم، ولكن تأخيره هاهنا في التعريف، والتعريف ليس بحسب الذات بل بحسب المفهوم.
وإن عنيتم به أن مفهوم المفرد مقدم على مفهوم المركب، فهو ممنوع; فإن القيود في مفهوم المركب وجوديّة، وفي مفهوم المفرد عدمية، والوجود في التصوّر سابق على العدم، فلذا أخر المفرد في التعريف وقدمه في الأقسام والأحكام لأنها بحسب الذات.
For if you say: The simple (mufrad) is naturally prior to the compound (murakkab), so why was it placed later in the definition? And violating natural order is near to error in the view of accomplished scholars. We say: For the simple and the compound there are two considerations: one according to essence (dhat), which is the extension of the simple like Zayd and Amr and others, and the second according to concept (mafhum), which is what the utterance is placed in correspondence to, like 'al-katib' (the writer) for example, for it has a concept which is 'a thing having writing', and an essence which is the extension of 'writer' among human individuals. If by your statement 'the simple is naturally prior to the compound' you mean that the essence of the simple is prior to the essence of the compound, then it is granted, but its placement later here in the definition (is) because definition is not according to essence but according to concept. And if you mean by it that the concept of the simple is prior to the concept of the compound, then it is denied; for the restrictions in the concept of the compound are existential, and in the concept of the simple are privative, and existence in conceptualization is prior to non-existence, therefore the simple was placed later in the definition and placed earlier in the divisions and rulings because they are according to essence.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange where an opponent raises an objection and the author responds. The objection notes that simple words are naturally prior to compound words (since compounds are built from simples), so why does the author define the compound first and the simple later? This seems to contradict natural order and approaches error. The author's response distinguishes two aspects: essence (dhat) and concept (mafhum). Essence refers to the external existents that the word applies to, while concept refers to the mental meaning that the word signifies. The opponent's claim that the simple is naturally prior can be interpreted in two ways. If they mean the essence of the simple is prior to the essence of the compound, this is conceded: individual simple words exist before compound expressions in reality. However, the definition here is concerned with concepts, not essences. If they mean the concept of the simple is prior to the concept of the compound, this is denied. The reason is that the concept of the compound involves positive restrictions (e.g., 'writer' has the restriction of having writing), while the concept of the simple is negative (it is defined by lacking what the compound has). Since the mind comprehends existence (positive attributes) before non-existence (privations), the compound's concept is conceptually prior. Therefore, it is appropriate to define the compound first and the simple later, based on conceptual order. However, in discussions of divisions and rulings (which involve external existence), the simple is placed first because in reality simples precede compounds. This shows the author's method: the order of definition follows logical conceptuality, not natural chronology.

Key Terms
ذات (dhat): Essence or external existence; the actual individuals that the word applies to, e.g., Zayd and Amr as extensions of mufrad. [Note: No significant shift; widely used in logic and philosophy.]
مفهوم (mafhum): The mental concept or intension that the word is placed to signify; the definition considers this aspect. [Note: No significant shift.]
طبعاً (tab'an): By nature or naturally; here referring to the natural order of existence where simples precede compounds. [Note: No significant shift.]
وضعاً (wad'an): By convention or by definition; the order in which terms are defined in this treatise. [Note: No significant shift.]

A) Because the essence of the simple is prior to the compound, so it should be defined later.
B) Because the concept of the compound involves existential restrictions, which are mentally prior to the privative concept of the simple.
C) Because the compound is more important in logic.
D) Because simple words are more ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The order of definition follows conceptual priority, where the compound is conceptually prior due to its positive restrictions, not natural existence priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Objection Against Basing Word Division on Mutabaqa
Objection against basing word division on mutabaqa meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that composition in necessary meaning does not entail composition in correspondence meaning.

Core Idea
Composition in necessary meaning can exist without composition in correspondence meaning.

The author has asserted that word division (individual/compound) should be based on correspondence meaning (mutabaqa) because necessary signification (iltizam) depends on correspondence. But an objector challenges this: why can't a word be compound in necessary meaning while simple in correspondence? This lesson unpacks that objection and its logical basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's initial argument
The author claims that if a part of a word indicates a part of its necessary meaning, it also indicates a part of the correspondence meaning, because necessary signification cannot exist without correspondence. So composition in necessary meaning would force composition in correspondence.
2
Find the objector's counterexample
The objector imagines a word whose parts indicate parts of a composite necessary meaning, but the correspondence meaning of the whole word is simple (not composite). For example, suppose 'roof-ceiling' (not a real Arabic compound, but illustrative) where the parts 'roof' and 'ceiling' together indicate a composite necessary meaning (e.g., 'covering above head'), but the correspondence meaning is simply 'ceiling'.
The objection relies on the possibility that necessary meaning is composite while correspondence meaning is not.
3
Understand why the objector thinks this refutes the author
If such a word exists, then composition in necessary meaning does not force composition in correspondence. The objector says there is no proof that it is impossible, so the author's argument fails.
This challenges the necessity of basing the division on mutabaqa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطابقي، لأن المعنى التضمّني جزء المعنى المطابقي، وجزء الجزء جزء. وأما في الالتزامي فلانه متي دل جزء اللفظ على جزء معناه الالتزامي بالالتزام، فقد دلّ على جزء المعنى المطابقي بالمطابقة، لامتناع تحقق (1) واعترض عليه بأن الدلالة الالتزامية وإن استلزمت المطابقة إلا أن تركيب اللفظ بحسب الالتزام لا يستلزم تركيبه بحسب المطابقة لجواز أن يكون المعنى الالتزامي مركباً يدل جزء اللفظ على جزئه ، ولا يكون المعنى المطابقي 5 ولا محذور في ذلك إذ لم يلزم حينئذ دلالة الالتزام بلا مطابقة، بل يلزم تركيب كذلك، المدلول الالتزامي دون المدلول المطابقي ؛ ولا دليل يدل على استحالة ذلك. ورة هذا الاعتراض بأن جزء اللفظ إذا دلّ على جزء معناه الالتزامي بالالتزام، فلابد أن يكون لهذا الجزء من اللفظ مدلول مطابقي، وإلا لزم ثبوت الالتزام بدون المطابقة، والجزء الآخر من اللفظ لا يكون مهملاً، وإلا لم يكن هناك تركيب - بل ضمّ مهمل إلى مستعمل - وإذا لم يكن مهملا - بل موضوعاً لمعنى - فذلك المعنى لا يكون عين المدلول المطابقي للجزء الأول، وإلاّ لكانا لفظين مترادفين يدل كلّ منهما على ما يدل عليه الآخر، فلا تركيب هناك أيضاً، بل يكون معنى مغايراً لمعنى الجزء الأول، فقد حصل لجزأي اللفظ مدلولان مطابقيان قطعاً، ولزم التركيب باعتبار المطابقة أيضاً. فإن قلت : إذا دل جزء اللفظ على جزء المعنى الالتزامي لايلزم أن تكون تلك الدلالة بالالتزام، لأن المعنى الالتزامي وإن كان خارجاً عن المعنى المطابقي إلا أنه لا يلزم أن تكون أجزاء المعنى الالتزامي خارجة عن المعنى المطابقي، وذلك لأن المركب من الداخل والخارج خارج. قلت : دلالته على جزء المعنى الالتزامي إما أن تكون التزامية أو تضمنية أو مطابقيّة، وعلى التقادير الثلاثة يثبت لذلك الجزء من اللفظ مدلول مطابقي ولابد أيضاً أن يكون للجزء الآخر من اللفظ مدلول مطابقي آخر - كما بيناه - فيلزم التركيب بحسب المطابقة قطعاً (شريف). الالتزام بدون المطابقة، وقد يتحقق الإفراد والتركيب بالنسبة إلى المعنى المطابقي لا بالنسبة إلى المعنى التضمني والالتزامي، كما في المثالين المذكورين، فلهذا خصص القسمة إلى الإفراد والتركيب بالمطابقة، إلا أن هذا الوجه يفيد أولويّة اعتبار المطابقة في القسمة، والوجه الأول- إن تم يفيد وجوب اعتبار المطابقة في القسمة.
The correspondence (meaning), because the inclusive meaning is part of the correspondence meaning, and a part of a part is a part. As for the necessary (meaning), whenever a part of an expression indicates a part of its necessary meaning by necessary signification, it has indicated a part of the correspondence meaning by correspondence, because it is impossible for necessary signification to occur without correspondence. (1) An objection was raised against it: that necessary signification, even if it entails correspondence, yet the composition of the expression in terms of necessary signification does not entail its composition in terms of correspondence, because it is possible that the necessary meaning is composite, and a part of the expression indicates its part, while the correspondence meaning is not [composite], and there is no problem in that, because then necessary signification without correspondence does not follow; rather, composition of the necessary indicated meaning follows without the correspondence indicated meaning, and there is no proof for the impossibility of that. The response to this objection: if a part of an expression indicates a part of its necessary meaning by necessary signification, then this part of the expression must have a correspondence indicated meaning; otherwise, necessary signification without correspondence would be established. And the other part of the expression cannot be insignificant (muhmal), otherwise there would be no composition—rather it would be joining an insignificant [part] to a used one. And if it is not insignificant but is assigned a meaning, then that meaning is not the same as the correspondence indicated meaning of the first part; otherwise they would be two synonymous expressions each indicating what the other indicates, so there would be no composition there either. Rather, it would be a meaning different from the meaning of the first part. Thus, the two parts of the expression have two correspondence indicated meanings necessarily, and composition in terms of correspondence follows as well. If you say: when a part of the expression indicates a part of the necessary meaning, it is not necessary that that signification be by necessary signification, because the necessary meaning, although external to the correspondence meaning, yet it is not necessary that the parts of the necessary meaning be external to the correspondence meaning, because the composite of internal and external is external. I say: its signification on the part of the necessary meaning is either necessary, or inclusive, or correspondence. On all three suppositions, that part of the expression has a correspondence indicated meaning, and the other part of the expression must also have another correspondence indicated meaning—as we have explained—so composition in terms of correspondence necessarily follows. (Sharif). The necessary (signification) without correspondence. And individual and composition may occur with respect to the correspondence meaning, not with respect to the inclusive and necessary meaning, as in the two examples mentioned. Therefore, the division into individual and compound was specified with respect to correspondence. However, this point indicates the precedence of considering correspondence in the division, while the first point—if sound—indicates the necessity of considering correspondence in the division.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion defending the logicians' decision to base the division of words into individual and compound on the correspondence meaning (al-mutabaqa) rather than on the inclusive (al-tazammun) or necessary (al-iltizam) meanings. The author begins by stating that the inclusive meaning is part of the correspondence meaning, so any division based on it would ultimately revert to correspondence. For the necessary meaning, the argument is that if a part of a word indicates a part of its necessary meaning by necessary signification, it also indicates a part of the correspondence meaning by correspondence, because necessary signification cannot exist without correspondence. An objector then raises a concern: necessary signification may entail correspondence, but a word's composition in terms of necessary meaning does not require its composition in terms of correspondence; the necessary meaning could be composite while the correspondence meaning is simple, and there is no proof against that. The author responds by analyzing the two parts of the word. If each part has a correspondence meaning—otherwise the word would not be composed—then both parts together yield two distinct correspondence meanings, forcing composition on the correspondence level as well. A further objection suggests that the part of the necessary meaning might itself be part of the correspondence meaning. The author refutes this by noting that the part's signification is either necessary, inclusive, or correspondence; in all cases, it has a correspondence meaning, and the other part also has a distinct correspondence meaning, so composition in correspondence is unavoidable. Finally, the author concludes that individual and compound can be realized with respect to correspondence meaning but not necessarily with inclusive or necessary meanings, as examples show. Thus the division is specified to correspondence. The first argument (if sound) shows necessity, the second shows priority.

The Author's Response: Parts Necessitate Correspondence Composition
Author's response to the objection, proving necessary composition implies correspondence composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that if parts of a word indicate parts of a necessary meaning, they must have correspondence meanings, forcing composition in correspondence as well.

Core Idea
Each part of a compound word has a correspondence meaning, and distinct parts have distinct correspondence meanings, so composition in correspondence is necessary.

The objector claimed composition in necessary meaning could exist without composition in correspondence. The author now provides a rigorous response: analyze the parts of the word. Each part must have a correspondence meaning, otherwise there would be no composition; and these correspondence meanings are distinct, so the word is also composite in correspondence. Let's walk through this analysis.

Guided Path
1
Assume a word with two parts indicating parts of a necessary meaning
Take a hypothetical compound word where part A indicates part X of the necessary meaning, and part B indicates part Y. The necessary meaning is composite (X + Y).
2
Check that each part has a correspondence meaning
If part A indicates X by necessary signification, then part A must have its own correspondence meaning, because necessary signification requires a prior correspondence meaning for the part itself. Without it, part A would be insignificant (muhmal) and the word would not be composed.
The author says: 'this part of the expression must have a correspondence indicated meaning; otherwise, necessary signification without correspondence would be established.'
3
Check that the parts have distinct correspondence meanings
If part A and part B had the same correspondence meaning, they would be synonyms and the word would not be a true compound (it would be two identical words). So their correspondence meanings must be different.
The author: 'that meaning is not the same as the correspondence indicated meaning of the first part; otherwise they would be two synonymous expressions... so there would be no composition.'
4
Conclude that the word is composite in correspondence
Since each part has its own distinct correspondence meaning, the word as a whole indicates a composite correspondence meaning (the combination of those two meanings). Thus, composition in necessary meaning forces composition in correspondence.
This refutes the objection.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion defending the logicians' decision to base the division of words into individual and compound on the correspondence meaning (al-mutabaqa) rather than on the inclusive (al-tazammun) or necessary (al-iltizam) meanings. The author begins by stating that the inclusive meaning is part of the correspondence meaning, so any division based on it would ultimately revert to correspondence. For the necessary meaning, the argument is that if a part of a word indicates a part of its necessary meaning by necessary signification, it also indicates a part of the correspondence meaning by correspondence, because necessary signification cannot exist without correspondence. An objector then raises a concern: necessary signification may entail correspondence, but a word's composition in terms of necessary meaning does not require its composition in terms of correspondence; the necessary meaning could be composite while the correspondence meaning is simple, and there is no proof against that. The author responds by analyzing the two parts of the word. If each part has a correspondence meaning—otherwise the word would not be composed—then both parts together yield two distinct correspondence meanings, forcing composition on the correspondence level as well. A further objection suggests that the part of the necessary meaning might itself be part of the correspondence meaning. The author refutes this by noting that the part's signification is either necessary, inclusive, or correspondence; in all cases, it has a correspondence meaning, and the other part also has a distinct correspondence meaning, so composition in correspondence is unavoidable. Finally, the author concludes that individual and compound can be realized with respect to correspondence meaning but not necessarily with inclusive or necessary meanings, as examples show. Thus the division is specified to correspondence. The first argument (if sound) shows necessity, the second shows priority.

Three Types of Single Words: Particle, Verb, Noun
Author defines the three types of single words and addresses objections about attached pronouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any single Arabic word as a particle, verb, or noun based on fitness for predication and indication of time.

Core Idea
A single word is either fit to be a predicate or not; if fit, it is a verb if it indicates time, otherwise a noun.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of single words in Islamic logic. The commentator explains the criteria by which any isolated word can be sorted into one of three categories: particle (adāt), verb (kalima), or noun (ism). Understanding this division is essential before tackling definitions and syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main branches of the division
Look at the initial statement: every single word is either fit to be predicated by itself or not. That is the first split. The unfitness branch gives the particle; the fitness branch is further split by time indication.
‘Fit to be predicated by itself’ means it can form a complete informative sentence when combined with another element (like a subject).
2
Distinguish verb from noun within the fit branch
Among words that are fit for predication, those whose form indicates a specific time (past, present, future) are verbs; those that do not are nouns.
In Arabic, verb forms carry tense morphology (e.g., ḍaraba vs. yadribu), whereas nouns like 'Zayd' or 'rajul' do not inherently specify time.
3
Apply the classification to example words
Test each word: 'fī' is not fit to be a predicate alone → particle. 'ḍaraba' is fit and its form indicates past time → verb. 'rajul' is fit but has no tense marker → noun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : اللفظ المفرد إما «أداة» أو «كلمة» أو «اسم»؛ لأنه إما أن يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده أو لا يصلح : فإن لم يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده فهو «الأداة» - كـ«في» و «لا». وإن صلح لذلك فإن دل بهيأته على زمان معين من الأزمنة الثلاثة فهو «الكلمة». وإن لم يدل فهو «الاسم». (1) يشكل هذا بمثل الضمائر المتصلة كالألف في «ضربا» والواو في «ضربوا» والكاف في «ضربك» والياء في «غلامي» فإنّ شيئاً من هذه الضمائر لا يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده. وربما يجاب عنه بأن المراد من عدم صلاحية الأداة لأن يخبر بها وحدها أنها لا تصلح لذلك ، لا بنفسها ولا بما يرادفها ، وتلك الضمائر تصلح لأن يخبر بما يرادفها ؛ فإن الألف في «ضربا» بمعنى «هما» والواوفي «ضربوا» بمعنى «هم» والكاف في «ضربك» بمعنى «أنت»، والياء في «غلامي» بمعنى «أنا»؛ وهذه المرادفات تصلح لأن يخبر بها وحدها، وليس لفظة «في» مرادفة للظرفية حتى يراد أنها لا تكون أداة أيضاً، وذلك لأن لفظ الظرفية معناها مطلق الظرفية، ولفظة «في » معناها ظرفيّة مخصوصة معتبرة بين حصول زيد وبين الدار، وهذه الظرفية المخصوصة المعتبرة على هذا الوجه لا تصلح لأن يخبر بها أو عنها، بخلاف معنى الظرفية المطلقة، فإنّه صالح لهما؛ وقس على ذلك معنى لفظ «الابتداء». ولو قيل : «الأداة ما لا يصلح لأن يخبر بها أو يخبر عنها» لم ترد الضمائر التي وقعت مخبراً عنها، كالألف والواو والتاء في «ضربت»؛ نعم يحتاج في «ضربك» و «غلامي» إلى التأويل المذكور. ولو قيل : «اللفظ المفرد إما أن لا يصلح معناه لأن يخبر به وعنه وحده الأداة» لم يحتج إلى تأويل، فإن الضمائر المتصلة المذكورة مما يصلح معناه لأن يخبر به وحده، وإن لم تصلح نفسها للإخبار به (شريف). قيل عليه : ليس المقصود من زيد في الدار» الإخبار عنه بالحصول مطلقاً، بل بالحصول في الدار، فلابد أن يكون لفظة «في » جزء من المخبر به في المعنى، فهو كما أنّ «لا» في «زيد لاحجر» جزء من أجزاء المخبر به، فلا فرق بينهما. وهذا كلام حق، لكن الشارح نظر إلى جانب اللفظ، فوجد الرفع الذي هو حق المخبر به في هذا التركيب حاصلاً في الجزء الآخر المقدر قبل كلمة «في»، فحكم بأن المخبريه قد تم قبلها، ووجد الرفع في «لا حجر» حاصلاً بعد «لا» فجعله جزء من المخبر به (شريف).
I say: The single word is either a 'particle' (adāt) or a 'verb' (kalima) or a 'noun' (ism); because it is either fit to be predicated by itself or not fit: If it is not fit to be predicated by itself then it is the 'particle' – like 'fī' and 'lā'. And if it is fit for that, then if it indicates by its form a specific time from the three times, then it is the 'verb'. And if it does not indicate, then it is the 'noun'. (1) This is problematic concerning attached pronouns like the 'alif' in 'ḍarabā' and the 'wāw' in 'ḍarabū' and the 'kāf' in 'ḍarabaka' and the 'yā'' in 'ghulāmī', for none of these pronouns is fit to be predicated by itself. And it is sometimes answered that what is meant by the particle's unfitness to be predicated alone is that it is not fit for that, neither by itself nor by what is synonymous with it, and those pronouns are fit to be predicated by what is synonymous with them; for the 'alif' in 'ḍarabā' means 'humā' and the 'wāw' in 'ḍarabū' means 'hum' and the 'kāf' in 'ḍarabaka' means 'anta', and the 'yā'' in 'ghulāmī' means 'anā'; and these synonyms are fit to be predicated alone, and the term 'fī' is not synonymous with 'ẓarfiyya' such that it would also be considered a particle, because the term 'ẓarfiyya' means absolute containment, and the term 'fī' means a specific containment considered between Zayd's being and the house, and this specific containment considered in this manner is not fit to be predicated by it or about it, unlike the meaning of absolute containment, for it is fit for both; and similarly for the meaning of the term 'al-ibtidā''. And if it were said: 'The particle is what is not fit to be predicated by it or predicated about it', then the pronouns that are predicated about – like the 'alif', 'wāw', and 'tā'' in 'ḍarabtu' – would not be included; yes, in 'ḍarabaka' and 'ghulāmī' the aforementioned interpretation is needed. And if it were said: 'The single word, if its meaning is not fit to be predicated by it and about it alone, is the particle', then no interpretation would be needed, for the aforementioned attached pronouns have meanings that are fit to be predicated alone, even if they themselves are not fit for predication (Sharīf). It was objected to him: The intended meaning of 'Zayd is in the house' is not predication about him of absolute being, but rather being in the house, so necessarily the term 'fī' is part of the predicate in meaning, just as 'lā' in 'Zayd is lā-ḥajar' is part of the parts of the predicate, so there is no difference between them. And this statement is true, but the commentator (al-shāriḥ) considered the aspect of the word, and found that the raf' which is the right of the predicate in this construction occurs in the other part estimated before the word 'fī', so he judged that predication has been completed before it, and he found the raf' in 'lā ḥajar' occurs after 'lā', so he made it part of the predicate (Sharīf).
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on a matn that divides single words into three categories: particle (adāt), verb (kalima), and noun (ism). The commentator begins by restating the classification: a single word is either fit to be predicated (i.e., to form a complete informative sentence) by itself or not. If not, it is a particle, like 'fī' (in) or 'lā' (no/not). If it is fit, then if its form indicates a specific tense (past, present, future), it is a verb; otherwise, it is a noun. An objection is raised: attached pronouns (ḍamā'ir muttaṣila) like the alif in 'ḍarabā' (they two hit) are single words but cannot stand alone as predicates, yet they are nouns or parts of verbs, not particles. The commentator provides several responses. First, the particle's unfitness applies to the word itself and its synonyms; attached pronouns have synonyms that can stand alone (e.g., 'humā', 'hum', 'anta', 'anā'), so they escape the definition. However, 'fī' has no such synonym—the concept of containment (ẓarfiyya) is broader, while 'fī' denotes a specific containment not fit for predication. A refined definition is offered: the particle is what cannot be predicated by it or about it (i.e., neither as subject nor predicate). This excludes pronouns when they are the subject (e.g., in 'ḍarabtu' the tā' is the subject/fā'i-l). A final formulation focuses on meaning rather than the word itself: if the meaning of the word is not fit to be predicated by it or about it, it is a particle. This avoids the problem entirely because attached pronouns have meanings that can be predicated. The commentator (al-Sharīf) then notes a counter-objection: in 'Zayd fī l-dār', the predicate is not simply 'being' but 'being in the house', so 'fī' is part of the predicate meaning, just like 'lā' in 'Zayd lā ḥajar' (Zayd is not a stone). The sharīḥ replies that grammatically, the raf' (nominative case) that indicates the subject-predicate relation is found on the estimated word (e.g., 'ḥāṣil' or 'kā'in') before 'fī', so the predication is complete before 'fī', whereas in 'lā ḥajar' the raf' appears on 'ḥajar' after 'lā', making 'lā' part of the predicate. This illustrates the interplay between logical and grammatical analysis in Islamic logic.

Key Takeaway
The primary division of single words is based on fitness for predication; within fit words, tense indication separates verbs from nouns.

Refining the Definition: Attached Pronouns and Synonyms
Author discusses objections to the classification from attached pronouns and offers multiple refinements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the refinements to the definition of particle (adāt) to correctly handle edge cases like attached pronouns.

Core Idea
The particle definition is refined to consider synonyms and meaning, preserving the classification against the challenge of attached pronouns.

The simple classification of single words seems to exclude attached pronouns like the 'alif' in 'ḍarabā' (they two hit). The commentator offers several refinements to the definition of 'particle' to ensure such pronouns remain nouns or verbs. This lesson walks through those refinements, showing how logical definitions are tested and improved.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Attached pronouns (e.g., '-ā' in 'ḍarabā') are single words that cannot stand alone as predicates. Yet they are considered nouns (or parts of verbs) and not particles. This challenges the initial definition.
The objection: if particle means 'not fit to be predicated alone', then these pronouns would be particles, which is incorrect.
2
Follow the first refinement: synonymity
The commentator says the particle's unfitness applies to itself and its synonyms. The pronoun '-ā' has the synonym 'humā' (they two), which can stand alone as a predicate. Therefore, the pronoun is not a particle. The particle 'fī' (in) has no such synonym.
Check: does 'fī' have a synonym that can be a predicate? No – 'ẓarfiyya' (containment) is a noun but has a broader meaning.
3
Examine the second refinement: scope of predication
Another formulation: the particle is what cannot be predicated by it or about it (i.e., cannot serve as subject or predicate). This excludes pronouns when they are the subject (e.g., 'tā'' in 'ḍarabtu' – 'I hit').
But pronouns like '-ka' in 'ḍarabaka' (he hit you) are objects, not subjects; they still need the synonym trick.
4
Consider the third refinement: meaning-based
The most refined definition: if the meaning of the word is not fit to be predicated alone, then it is a particle. Attached pronouns have meanings (like 'humā' or 'anta') that are fit for predication, even if the word itself is not.
This avoids any special pleading: the meaning of 'fī' (specific containment) is not fit for predication, so it remains a particle.

أقول : اللفظ المفرد إما «أداة» أو «كلمة» أو «اسم»؛ لأنه إما أن يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده أو لا يصلح : فإن لم يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده فهو «الأداة» - كـ«في» و «لا». وإن صلح لذلك فإن دل بهيأته على زمان معين من الأزمنة الثلاثة فهو «الكلمة». وإن لم يدل فهو «الاسم». (1) يشكل هذا بمثل الضمائر المتصلة كالألف في «ضربا» والواو في «ضربوا» والكاف في «ضربك» والياء في «غلامي» فإنّ شيئاً من هذه الضمائر لا يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده. وربما يجاب عنه بأن المراد من عدم صلاحية الأداة لأن يخبر بها وحدها أنها لا تصلح لذلك ، لا بنفسها ولا بما يرادفها ، وتلك الضمائر تصلح لأن يخبر بما يرادفها ؛ فإن الألف في «ضربا» بمعنى «هما» والواوفي «ضربوا» بمعنى «هم» والكاف في «ضربك» بمعنى «أنت»، والياء في «غلامي» بمعنى «أنا»؛ وهذه المرادفات تصلح لأن يخبر بها وحدها، وليس لفظة «في» مرادفة للظرفية حتى يراد أنها لا تكون أداة أيضاً، وذلك لأن لفظ الظرفية معناها مطلق الظرفية، ولفظة «في » معناها ظرفيّة مخصوصة معتبرة بين حصول زيد وبين الدار، وهذه الظرفية المخصوصة المعتبرة على هذا الوجه لا تصلح لأن يخبر بها أو عنها، بخلاف معنى الظرفية المطلقة، فإنّه صالح لهما؛ وقس على ذلك معنى لفظ «الابتداء». ولو قيل : «الأداة ما لا يصلح لأن يخبر بها أو يخبر عنها» لم ترد الضمائر التي وقعت مخبراً عنها، كالألف والواو والتاء في «ضربت»؛ نعم يحتاج في «ضربك» و «غلامي» إلى التأويل المذكور. ولو قيل : «اللفظ المفرد إما أن لا يصلح معناه لأن يخبر به وعنه وحده الأداة» لم يحتج إلى تأويل، فإن الضمائر المتصلة المذكورة مما يصلح معناه لأن يخبر به وحده، وإن لم تصلح نفسها للإخبار به (شريف). قيل عليه : ليس المقصود من زيد في الدار» الإخبار عنه بالحصول مطلقاً، بل بالحصول في الدار، فلابد أن يكون لفظة «في » جزء من المخبر به في المعنى، فهو كما أنّ «لا» في «زيد لاحجر» جزء من أجزاء المخبر به، فلا فرق بينهما. وهذا كلام حق، لكن الشارح نظر إلى جانب اللفظ، فوجد الرفع الذي هو حق المخبر به في هذا التركيب حاصلاً في الجزء الآخر المقدر قبل كلمة «في»، فحكم بأن المخبريه قد تم قبلها، ووجد الرفع في «لا حجر» حاصلاً بعد «لا» فجعله جزء من المخبر به (شريف).
I say: The single word is either a 'particle' (adāt) or a 'verb' (kalima) or a 'noun' (ism); because it is either fit to be predicated by itself or not fit: If it is not fit to be predicated by itself then it is the 'particle' – like 'fī' and 'lā'. And if it is fit for that, then if it indicates by its form a specific time from the three times, then it is the 'verb'. And if it does not indicate, then it is the 'noun'. (1) This is problematic concerning attached pronouns like the 'alif' in 'ḍarabā' and the 'wāw' in 'ḍarabū' and the 'kāf' in 'ḍarabaka' and the 'yā'' in 'ghulāmī', for none of these pronouns is fit to be predicated by itself. And it is sometimes answered that what is meant by the particle's unfitness to be predicated alone is that it is not fit for that, neither by itself nor by what is synonymous with it, and those pronouns are fit to be predicated by what is synonymous with them; for the 'alif' in 'ḍarabā' means 'humā' and the 'wāw' in 'ḍarabū' means 'hum' and the 'kāf' in 'ḍarabaka' means 'anta', and the 'yā'' in 'ghulāmī' means 'anā'; and these synonyms are fit to be predicated alone, and the term 'fī' is not synonymous with 'ẓarfiyya' such that it would also be considered a particle, because the term 'ẓarfiyya' means absolute containment, and the term 'fī' means a specific containment considered between Zayd's being and the house, and this specific containment considered in this manner is not fit to be predicated by it or about it, unlike the meaning of absolute containment, for it is fit for both; and similarly for the meaning of the term 'al-ibtidā''. And if it were said: 'The particle is what is not fit to be predicated by it or predicated about it', then the pronouns that are predicated about – like the 'alif', 'wāw', and 'tā'' in 'ḍarabtu' – would not be included; yes, in 'ḍarabaka' and 'ghulāmī' the aforementioned interpretation is needed. And if it were said: 'The single word, if its meaning is not fit to be predicated by it and about it alone, is the particle', then no interpretation would be needed, for the aforementioned attached pronouns have meanings that are fit to be predicated alone, even if they themselves are not fit for predication (Sharīf). It was objected to him: The intended meaning of 'Zayd is in the house' is not predication about him of absolute being, but rather being in the house, so necessarily the term 'fī' is part of the predicate in meaning, just as 'lā' in 'Zayd is lā-ḥajar' is part of the parts of the predicate, so there is no difference between them. And this statement is true, but the commentator (al-shāriḥ) considered the aspect of the word, and found that the raf' which is the right of the predicate in this construction occurs in the other part estimated before the word 'fī', so he judged that predication has been completed before it, and he found the raf' in 'lā ḥajar' occurs after 'lā', so he made it part of the predicate (Sharīf).
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharḥ) on a matn that divides single words into three categories: particle (adāt), verb (kalima), and noun (ism). The commentator begins by restating the classification: a single word is either fit to be predicated (i.e., to form a complete informative sentence) by itself or not. If not, it is a particle, like 'fī' (in) or 'lā' (no/not). If it is fit, then if its form indicates a specific tense (past, present, future), it is a verb; otherwise, it is a noun. An objection is raised: attached pronouns (ḍamā'ir muttaṣila) like the alif in 'ḍarabā' (they two hit) are single words but cannot stand alone as predicates, yet they are nouns or parts of verbs, not particles. The commentator provides several responses. First, the particle's unfitness applies to the word itself and its synonyms; attached pronouns have synonyms that can stand alone (e.g., 'humā', 'hum', 'anta', 'anā'), so they escape the definition. However, 'fī' has no such synonym—the concept of containment (ẓarfiyya) is broader, while 'fī' denotes a specific containment not fit for predication. A refined definition is offered: the particle is what cannot be predicated by it or about it (i.e., neither as subject nor predicate). This excludes pronouns when they are the subject (e.g., in 'ḍarabtu' the tā' is the subject/fā'i-l). A final formulation focuses on meaning rather than the word itself: if the meaning of the word is not fit to be predicated by it or about it, it is a particle. This avoids the problem entirely because attached pronouns have meanings that can be predicated. The commentator (al-Sharīf) then notes a counter-objection: in 'Zayd fī l-dār', the predicate is not simply 'being' but 'being in the house', so 'fī' is part of the predicate meaning, just like 'lā' in 'Zayd lā ḥajar' (Zayd is not a stone). The sharīḥ replies that grammatically, the raf' (nominative case) that indicates the subject-predicate relation is found on the estimated word (e.g., 'ḥāṣil' or 'kā'in') before 'fī', so the predication is complete before 'fī', whereas in 'lā ḥajar' the raf' appears on 'ḥajar' after 'lā', making 'lā' part of the predicate. This illustrates the interplay between logical and grammatical analysis in Islamic logic.

Key Takeaway
Logical definitions must be tested against edge cases; refined definitions that consider meanings rather than mere words are more robust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining Tool Words (Adawāt) in Logic
Author defines adawāt via two examples and addresses objections about pronouns and null verbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical criteria for classifying a word as an adāh (tool) and apply these criteria to distinguish tools from non-tools in Arabic sentences.

Core Idea
A tool word is one whose meaning cannot be predicated alone or be the subject of predication.

In the previous lesson, we discussed the components of a proposition. Now we turn to a key logical term—the adāh (tool). The author defines it through examples, showing that 'fī' and 'lā' are tools, but pronouns and null verbs require careful handling. This lesson will walk you through the author's criteria and the objections raised, equipping you to identify tools in any Arabic expression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two examples
The author gives 'fī' (in) and 'lā' (not) as tools. For 'fī', the real predicate is an estimated word like 'present'; 'fī' itself cannot be a predicate. For 'lā', it can be part of a predicate but cannot stand alone as a predicate (e.g., 'lā ḥajar' is the predicate).
2
Analyse the refinement: pronouns
Pronouns like 'kāf' in 'ḍarbuka' (meaning 'you') or 'yā' in 'ghulāmī' (meaning 'I') are not tools because their meanings can be predicated alone, even if the word itself is attached. The author emphasizes that the criterion is based on meaning, not word form.
3
Understand the objection about 'fī'
An objection says that 'fī' is part of the predicate in meaning (e.g., 'in the house' is the predicate). The commentator (Sharīf) resolves this by noting that at the word level, the grammatical sign of predication (raf') appears before 'fī', so the predication is complete before it. Hence 'fī' is not part of the predicate at the word level.
4
Address the null verbs objection
Null verbs like 'kāna' cannot be predicated alone, so would they be tools? The author affirms this, noting that logicians divide tools into temporal and non-temporal, with null verbs being temporal tools. This differs from grammar, where null verbs are considered verbs, not particles.

He only mentioned two examples because that which is not suitable to be predicated alone is either not suitable for predication at all, like 'fī' (in), for the predicate in our saying 'Zayd is in the house' is 'present' or 'existing', and 'fī' has no part in the predication; or it is suitable for predication but not suitable to be predicated alone, like 'lā' (not), for the predicate in our saying 'Zayd is not-stone' is 'not-stone', so it has no part in the predication. Perhaps you say: The null verbs are not suitable to be predicated alone, so they must be tools. We say: There is no difficulty in that; indeed they divided tools into temporal and non-temporal, and temporal ones are the null verbs. The utmost in the chapter is that their terminology does not match the grammarians' terminology, and that is not necessary, because they look at meaning, while the grammarians look at the word itself. (1) Meaning that the people at the beginning of the chapter on propositions mentioned that the copula between subject and predicate is a tool, and they divided the copula into non-temporal—which does not indicate time at all, like 'huwa' in your saying 'Zayd huwa standing'—and temporal—which indicates it, like 'kāna' in 'Zayd was standing'. So that indicates that they considered the null verbs as tools (Sharīf).
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion of the term 'adawāt' (tool words) in logic. The author begins by noting that two examples are given because a word that cannot serve as predicate alone either has no capacity for predication at all (like 'fī' in 'Zayd is in the house' where the real predicate is 'present') or can be predicated but not alone (like 'lā' in 'Zayd is not-stone' where 'lā' is part of the predicate phrase). The author then clarifies that pronouns like 'hum', 'kāf', and 'yā' are synonyms and can stand alone as predicates, unlike 'fī' which is a specific circumstantial relation and thus not suitable as a predicate. The author further refines the definition: if one says a tool is that which cannot be predicated by or about, some pronouns (like the attached pronouns in 'ḍarabtu') would qualify as tools, but they are not because their meaning can be predicated alone even if the word itself cannot. The true definition is that a tool is a word whose meaning cannot serve alone as predicate or subject of predication. The author addresses an objection that 'fī' is part of the meaning of the predicate, and responds that the commentator (shāriḥ) looks at the word level: the predicate's grammatical sign (raf') appears before 'fī' in the sentence, so predication is complete before it, whereas for 'lā', the raf' appears after it, so 'lā' is part of the predicate. Finally, the author addresses the objection that null verbs (like 'kāna') cannot be predicated alone and thus should be tools. The author agrees there is no problem; in fact, logicians divided tools into temporal and non-temporal, with null verbs being temporal tools. The difference from grammar is simply that logicians consider meaning while grammarians consider word form. A gloss from Sharīf confirms that in the chapter on propositions, the logicians treated the copula as a tool and explicitly included null verbs as temporal copulas.

The Fourfold Division of Singular Words
Author presents the logicians' division of singular words into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any singular word into one of the four categories: tool, incomplete verb, verb, or noun.

Core Idea
Two criteria define the division: completeness of meaning and indication of time.

In logic, before analyzing propositions, we must first understand the types of individual words. The logicians divided singular words based on whether their meaning is complete enough to stand alone in predication, and whether they indicate time. This lesson walks through the four resulting categories and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two criteria
First, grasp the two axes: (1) Is the meaning complete, i.e., suitable for being a subject or predicate alone? (2) Does the word's form indicate time?
2
Identify the first division: complete vs incomplete
Singular words either have a complete meaning that can be predicated or be a subject, or an incomplete meaning that cannot function alone in predication.
The author says: 'اللفظ المفرد إما أن يكون معناه غير تام - أي لا يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده ولا عنه - وإما أن يكون معناه تاماً'.
3
Subdivide incomplete words
Among incomplete-meaning words, if the word does not indicate time at all, it is a 'tool' (adāh). If it indicates time, it is an 'incomplete verb' (like kāna).
Tools: e.g., the copula 'huwa'. Incomplete verbs: e.g., 'kāna'.
4
Subdivide complete words
Among complete-meaning words, if the word does not indicate time by its form, it is a 'noun' (ism). If it does indicate time, it is a 'verb' (kalimah).
Nouns: e.g., 'Zaid'. Verbs: e.g., 'yaqūmu' (he stands).
5
Handle special cases: relative nouns
Relative nouns (al-mawsūlāt) like 'alladhī' seem incomplete, but the author argues they are complete nouns that need a relative clause only to clarify their reference, not to complete their meaning.
Thus they are not tools but nouns.

(۱) يعني أن القوم في أول باب القضايا ذكروا أن الرابط بين الموضوع والمحمول أداة، وقسموا الرابطة إلى غير زمانية - وهي ما لا يدلّ على زمان أصلاً، كـ«هو» في قولك «زيد هو قائم» - وإلى زمانية - وهي مايدل عليه، كـ«كان» في «زيد كان قائماً، فدل ذلك على أنهم عدّوا الأفعال الناقصة أدوات (شريف). (۲) لأن مقصودهم تصحيح الألفاظ، فلما وجدوا الأفعال الناقصة أنها تشارك ما عداها من الأفعال المسماة بالتامة - لتمامها مع فاعلها كلاماً - في كثير من العلامات والأحوال اللفظية، جعلوها أفعالاً؛ وأما القوم فقد وجدوها أن معانيها توافق معاني الأدوات في عدم صلاحية الإخبار بها وحدها، أدرجوها في الأدوات وإن كانت ممتازة عن سائر الأدوات بالدلالة على الزمان، ولذلك سماها بعضهم كلمات وجودية لأنها تدلّ على الثبوت؛ ومن ثم قيل : الأولى أن تربّع القسمة ويقال : اللفظ المفرد إما أن يكون معناه غير تام - أي لا يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده ولا عنه - وإما أن يكون معناه تاماً - أي يصلح أولهما معاً - والأول - أعني الغير التام - إما أن لا يدل على زمان أصلاً، فهو «الأداة» وإما أن يدلّ عليه، فهو الأفعال الناقصة؛ والثاني أيضاً إن لم يدل على زمان بهيئته ، فهو «الاسم» وإن دلّ فهو الكلمة. وقد يقال أيضاً : الأسماء الموصولة لا تصلح لأن يخبربها وحدها، بل تحتاج إلى الصلة في ذاتها، فيجب أن تكون أدوات. ويجاب : بأنها صالحة لذلك، لكنها لإبهامها تحتاج إلى صلة تبينها؛ فالمحكوم به وعليه هو الموصول، والصلة خارجة عنه مبينة له (شريف).
(1) He means that the people (logicians) at the beginning of the chapter on propositions mentioned that the copula between subject and predicate is a tool, and they divided the copula into non-temporal - which indicates no time at all, like 'huwa' in your saying 'Zaid huwa standing' - and temporal - which indicates it, like 'kāna' in 'Zaid kāna standing'. That indicates that they considered the incomplete verbs as tools (Sharif). (2) Because their purpose is to correct the words. When they found that the incomplete verbs share with the other verbs called complete - because they form a complete statement with their subject - many verbal signs and states, they made them verbs. As for the people (logicians), they found that their meanings agree with the meanings of tools in lacking suitability for predication alone; they included them among tools, even though they are distinguished from other tools by indicating time. Therefore, some called them existential words because they indicate existence. Hence it was said: It is better to make a fourfold division and say: The singular word either its meaning is incomplete - i.e., not suitable for predication alone nor about it - or its meaning is complete - i.e., suitable for both together. The first - the incomplete - either does not indicate time at all, so it is the 'tool', or indicates it, so it is the 'incomplete verbs'; and the second also, if it does not indicate time by its form, then it is the 'noun', and if it does indicate, then it is the 'word' (verb). And it may also be said: The relative nouns are not suitable for predication alone, but need the relative clause in themselves, so they must be tools. And it is answered: That they are suitable for that, but due to their ambiguity they need a relative clause to clarify them; so the subject and predicate are the relative pronoun, and the relative clause is external to it, clarifying it (Sharif).
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (attributed to Sharif) that explains the logicians' classification of words. It begins by noting that the logicians consider the copula (rābiṭ) a tool and divide it into temporal (like kāna) and non-temporal (like huwa). This shows they treat incomplete verbs as tools. The commentary then explains why grammarians treat incomplete verbs as verbs (due to verbal characteristics), while logicians treat them as tools (due to meaning dependency). The author then proposes a comprehensive fourfold division: singular words are either incomplete in meaning (not suitable for independent predication) or complete. Incomplete words divide into tools (no time indication) and incomplete verbs (indicate time). Complete words divide into verbs (indicate time by form) and nouns (no time indication). An additional objection about relative nouns is raised and answered: relative nouns can stand alone but need a relative clause to clarify their reference.

الكلمة

Distinguishing Noun and Verb by Time Indication
Definition of noun and verb by time indication and defense of relative nouns as nouns.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a noun from a verb in Arabic based on the logical criterion of time indication and resolve the apparent exception of relative nouns.

Core Idea
A verb indicates time by its form; a noun does not—even relative nouns are still nouns.

In this passage, the author defines the noun (اسم) and verb (كلمة) not by grammatical tradition but by a logical criterion: whether the word's form alone signals a specific time. This definition helps us classify words in logical propositions. But what about relative nouns like 'who'? An objection is raised and answered, showing that they remain nouns despite needing a clarifying clause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion
Find the key phrase: 'indicates by its form a specific time'. That is the differentia between noun and verb.
2
Apply the criterion to examples
Test 'daraba' and 'yadribu': they have forms indicating past and present. So they are verbs. Test 'Zayd' and 'Amr': no time indication, so they are nouns.
3
Understand the objection
Relative nouns (e.g., 'alladhi') cannot stand alone as predicates without a relative clause. The objector says they must be particles, not nouns.
4
Analyze the response
The author argues that relative nouns are suitable as predicates but require clarification due to ambiguity; the clause is external, so the relative noun remains the subject/predicate.
5
Evaluate the definition's completeness
Check if all nouns and verbs satisfy the criterion. The relative noun case is handled, confirming the definition works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند تغاير جهتي البحثين لا يلزم تطابق الاصطلاحين. وإن صلح لأن يخبر به وحده، فإما أن يدل بهيأته وصيغته على زمان معين من الأزمنة الثلاثة - كـ«ضرب» و «يضرب» - فهو الكلمة، أو لا يدل، فهو الاسم كـ«زيد» و «عمرو». وقد يقال أيضاً: الأسماء الموصولة لا تصلح لأن يخبر بها وحدها، بل تحتاج إلى الصلة في ذاتها، فيجب أن تكون أدوات. ويجاب: بأنها صالحة لذلك، لكنها لإبهامها تحتاج إلى صلة تبينها؛ فالمحكوم به وعليه هو الموصول، والصلة خارجة عنه مبينة له (شريف).
And when the two aspects of the two investigations differ, the correspondence of the two terminologies is not necessary. And if it [a word] is suitable to be used alone as a predicate, then either it indicates by its form and pattern a specific time among the three times - like 'daraba' and 'yadribu' - then it is the verb, or it does not indicate, then it is the noun, like 'Zayd' and 'Amr'. It may also be said: relative nouns are not suitable to be used alone as predicates, rather they need in themselves the relative clause, so they must be particles. The answer is: they are suitable for that, but due to their ambiguity they need a relative clause to clarify them; so the subject and predicate [of the judgment] is the relative noun, and the relative clause is external to it clarifying it (Sharif).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage defines the noun (اسم) and the verb (كلمة) in terms of their ability to indicate time through their form. The author first notes that when the perspectives of the two investigations differ (perhaps referring to grammar and logic), the terminologies need not correspond. Then he gives the criterion: if a word can stand alone as a predicate, it is either a verb if its form indicates a specific time (past, present, future), or a noun if it does not. Examples are given: ضرب (he struck) and يضرب (he strikes) are verbs; زيد and عمرو are nouns. An objection is raised concerning relative nouns like 'who' (الموصول), which apparently cannot stand alone as predicates because they need a relative clause. The answer clarifies that they can stand alone but due to ambiguity they require a clarifying clause; the judgment's subject and predicate remain the relative noun itself, and the clause is external. This preserves the classification of relative nouns as nouns, not particles.

Key Terms
اسم (ism): A word that can be used as a predicate alone and does not indicate a specific time by its form. [Note: No significant shift.]
كلمة (kalima): A word that can be used as a predicate alone and indicates a specific time by its form; in this context synonym for فعل (verb). [Note: In Arabic grammar, كلمة includes nouns, verbs, and particles; here it is restricted to verbs.]
هيأة (hay'a): The form or structure of a word, including arrangement, vowels, and quiescence. [Note: No significant shift.]
صيغة (sigha): The pattern or morphological template of a word. [Note: No significant shift.]

Definition of Form (Hay'a) and the Objection on Non-Arabic Languages
Author defines الهيأة and explains why the verb's indication of time must be by form, then addresses the objection that this applies only to Arabic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define الهيأة (form) and distinguish between words that indicate time by form versus by matter, and explain the objection about Arabic specificity and the response to it.

Core Idea
A verb indicates time through its changing form, not its fixed matter—but is this only true in Arabic?

In this passage, the author refines the definition of a verb (كلمة) by introducing the concept of الهيأة (form) as distinct from المادة (matter). You will see why some time words like 'yesterday' are not verbs, and then confront a challenge: the verb's form-based time indication seems to work only in Arabic. Let's unpack the definition and the defense.

Guided Path
1
Define الهيأة (form)
Look at the definition: it is the form that results from the arrangement, precedence, delay, vowels, and quiescences of the letters. This is the 'image' of the word, while the letters themselves are the 'matter'.
Note that الهيأة and صيغة are used synonymously here.
2
Understand the restriction of the verb definition
The verb was previously defined as indicating time. That definition is now restricted: it must indicate time by its form, not by its matter. Words like زمان (time), أمس (yesterday), يوم (day) indicate time by their inherent lexical meaning (matter), not by a changing pattern.
Example: ضرب (past) and يضرب (present) share the root ض-ر-ب but differ in form, showing time indication by form.
3
Identify the objection
Find the objection: if this property relies on form, it may be true only in Arabic. Evidence: Persian words 'آمد' (came) and 'آيد' (comes) are identical in form but differ in time, contradicting the claim.
The objection challenges the universality of the definition, since logic/mantiq deals with universal concepts.
4
Analyze the answer
The response acknowledges the universal scope of mantiq but argues that since the science is primarily recorded in Arabic, it is acceptable for some specifics to be confined to Arabic. The priority is Arabic, so the definition stands for the language of the discipline.
This is a pragmatic defense: the rule holds for the language in which the science is conducted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالهيأة والصيغة الهيأة الحاصلة للحروف باعتبار تقديمها وتأخيرها وحركاتها وسكناتها، وهي صورة الكلمة، والحروف مادتها. وإنما قيد حد الكلمة بها لإخراج ما يدل على الزمان لا بهيأته – بل بحسب جوهره ومادته - كالزمان والأمس واليوم والصبوح والغبق فإن دلالتها على الزمان بموادها وجواهرها لا بهيآتها، بخلاف الكلمة فإن دلالتها على الزمان بحسب هيأتها، بشهادة اختلاف الزمان عند اختلاف الهيأة وإن اتحدت المادة كضرب ويضرب. واعترض عليه بأن دلالة الكلمة على الزمان بالصيغة - أن صحت - فإنما تصح في لغة العرب دون لغة العجم، فإن قولك: «آمد» و«آيد» متحدان في الصيغة ومختلفان في الزمان، وقد تقدم أن نظر الفن في الألفاظ على وجه كلي غير مخصوص بلغة دون أخرى. وأجيب بأن الاهتمام باللغة العربية - التي دون بها الفن غالباً في زماننا أكثر، فلا بعد في اختصاص بعض الأحوال بهذه اللغة - كما مرت إليه الإشارة (شريف).
And by 'form' and 'pattern' is meant the form that results for letters in terms of their precedence and delay, movements and quiescences, which is the image of the word, while the letters are its matter. The definition of 'word' (verb) was restricted by this to exclude what indicates time not by its form—but according to its essence and matter—like 'zaman' (time), 'ams' (yesterday), 'yawm' (day), 'subuh' (morning), and 'ghabaq' (evening-drink). Their indication of time is by their matters and essences, not by their forms. But the verb's indication of time is according to its form, as attested by the difference of time when the form differs even if the matter is the same, like 'daraba' (he struck) and 'yadribu' (he strikes). It was objected that the verb's indication of time by pattern—if correct—is correct only in the Arabic language, not in non-Arabic languages. For example, 'āmad' and 'āyid' are identical in form but different in time, and it was previously stated that the inquiry of this art concerning words is universal, not restricted to one language. The answer is that the concern with the Arabic language—in which the art is predominantly recorded in our time—is greater, so there is no impossibility in some conditions being specific to this language, as has been previously indicated (Sharif).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines الهيأة (form) as the arrangement, vowelization, and consonantality of the letters, which is the form of a word, while the letters themselves are its matter. The definition of verb (كلمة) is restricted by this form to exclude words that indicate time by their matter, not by their form, such as 'zaman' (time), 'ams' (yesterday), 'yawm' (day), etc. These indicate time inherently through their lexical meaning, not through a paradigmatic pattern. In contrast, verbs indicate time by their varying forms, as seen in 'daraba' (past) and 'yadribu' (present/future), where the matter (root d-r-b) is the same but the form changes with time. An objection is raised: this property of indicating time by form is true only in Arabic, not in other languages like Persian, where 'āmad' and 'āyid' have the same form but different times. The answer acknowledges the universal nature of logic but notes that since this science is predominantly recorded in Arabic, it is acceptable for some specifics to be confined to Arabic.

Logical Parts of Speech: Ism, Kalima, Ada
Author defines the three logical parts of speech and distinguishes them from grammatical categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three logical categories of words (ism, kalima, ada) based on their predication function and apply this classification to Arabic examples.

Core Idea
Logicians classify words by their role in predication, not by grammar.

In this passage, the author introduces the logicians' own tripartite division of expressions, independent of the grammarian's categories. You will learn how each type is defined by its ability to serve as subject or predicate in a proposition. The classification revolves around the concept of 'predication' (ism can be subject and predicate, kalima can be predicate only, ada cannot stand alone in predication).

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection about composition
The passage begins by addressing a potential objection: since a kalima (e.g., 'daraba') has a root indicating an event and a form indicating time, it might be considered composite. Note the author's response: composition requires parts arranged sequentially (like letters in a word), not the simultaneous presence of matter and form.
This clarifies that kalima is not composite in the sense relevant to logical analysis.
2
Extract the three logical definitions
Locate the definitions: ada is a word whose meaning cannot truly be predicated alone; kalima can be predicated alone but cannot be the subject of predication; ism can serve both as subject and predicate. These are functional definitions based on semantics, not morphology.
Pay attention to the phrase 'yukhbar bihi' (predicated about) vs 'yukhbar anhu' (predicated of).
3
Apply the criteria to examples
Test the classification: 'idha' (if) is grammatically a noun but logically an ada; 'kana' (was) is grammatically a verb but logically an ada because it cannot stand alone as a predicate; 'daraba' (he hit) is a kalima because it can be a predicate (e.g., 'Zaydun daraba') but not a subject. Contrast with 'Zaydun' which is an ism.
4
Understand the conditions of distinction
The author describes the distinctions using technical terms: the ada is distinguished from its siblings by a 'cognitive condition' ('qayd ilmi'), the kalima by an 'existential condition' ('qayd wujudi'), and the ism by two existential conditions. This reflects the logical structure of the definitions.
These conditions are not essential for basic application but deepen understanding.
5
Reflect on the etymological names
Each name has a rationale: 'ada' means tool (it helps combine words), 'kalima' derives from 'kalim' (wound) because its indication of time wounds the mind with change, and 'ism' comes from 'sumuww' (height) because it is the highest category of words.
This mnemonic reinforces the logical hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مباحث الألفاظ
اتحاد الهيأة وإن اختلفت المادة، كضرب وطلب). فإن قلت: فعلى هذا يلزم أن تكون الكلمة مركبة، لدلالة أصلها ومادتها على الحدث وهيأتها وصورتها على الزمان، فيكون جزؤها دالاً على جزء معناها. فنقول: المعني من التركيب أن يكون هناك أجزاء مترتبة الألفاظ الثلاث والحروف)، والهيأة مع المادة ليست بهذه المثابة. وبالجملة كل ما لا يصلح معناه حقيقة لأن يخبر به وحده فهو عند القوم «أداة» سواء كان عند النحاة فعلاً - كالأفعال الناقصة – أو اسماً - كـ«إذا » ونظائرها - وكلّ ما يصلح لأن يخبر به وحده ولا يصلح لأن يخبر عنه فهو عندهم «كلمة» وإن كان عند النحاة من الأسماء، فعلى هذا يكون امتياز الأداة عن أخويها بقيد علمي، وامتياز الكلمة عنها بقيد وجودي، وعن الاسم بقيد عدمي، وامتياز الاسم عنهما بقيدين وجوديين. وأما تسميتها بالأداة فلأنّها آلة في تركيب الألفاظ بعضها مع بعض. وأما بالكلمة فلأنها من الكلم - وهو الجرح كأنها لما دلّت على الزمان وهو متجدّد ومتصرّم تكلم الخاطر بتغيير معناها. وأما بالاسم فلأنه أعلى مرتبة من سائر أنواع الألفاظ فيكون مشتملاً على معني السمو، وهو العلوّ.
Subjects of expressions. The combination of form even if matter differs, like 'daraba' and 'talaba'. If you say: according to this, the word (kalima) would have to be composite, because its root and matter indicate the event and its form and shape indicate time, so its part indicates part of its meaning. We say: the meaning of composition is that there are parts arranged in order (the three words and letters), and the form together with the matter is not of this status. In summary, everything whose meaning truly does not suffice to be predicated alone is according to them a 'tool', whether according to grammarians it is a verb – like incomplete verbs – or a noun – like 'idha' and its analogues. And everything that suffices to be predicated alone but does not suffice to be predicated about is according to them a 'word', even if according to grammarians it is among nouns. So on this basis, the distinction of the tool from its two siblings is by a cognitive condition, and the distinction of the word from it is by an existential condition, and from the noun by a privative condition, and the distinction of the noun from them is by two existential conditions. As for naming it 'tool', because it is an instrument in combining expressions with each other. As for 'word', because it is from 'kalim' – which is wound – as if because it indicates time, which is renewing and passing, the mind is affected by changing its meaning. As for 'noun', because it is of a higher rank than the other types of expressions, so it contains the meaning of height, which is loftiness.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines the three logical categories of verbal expressions: ism (noun), kalima (word/verb), and ada (tool/particle). The author explains that logicians classify words based on their semantic function in predication, not according to grammatical categories. The passage begins by addressing an objection: if a kalima (like a verb) has a root indicating an event and a form indicating time, wouldn't it be composite? The reply clarifies that composition requires sequentially perceived parts, which does not apply to the simultaneous presence of matter and form. Then a comprehensive definition is given: an ada is anything whose meaning cannot truly stand alone in predication (like particles and incomplete verbs); a kalima can stand alone as a predicate but cannot be the subject of predication (like verbs); an ism can serve both as subject and predicate (like nouns and adjectives). The author further explains that the distinction is based on cognitive, existential, and privative conditions. Finally, the reasons for the names are provided: 'ada' because it is an instrument, 'kalima' because it 'wounds' the mind with temporal change, and 'ism' because it signifies loftiness. The passage emphasizes that logical classification is independent of grammatical categories; for example, some words grammarians call nouns are considered 'kalima' by logicians if they indicate time, like 'idha'.

Key Terms
اسم
كلمة
أداة

Key Takeaway
Logicians classify words into ism, kalima, and ada based solely on their ability to function as subject or predicate in a proposition, independent of grammatical categories.
