Definition of Universal and Particular: The Condition of 'Nafs al-Tasawwur'
Author defines universal and particular with the qualification 'nafs al-tasawwur' and discusses hypothetical universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define universal and particular using the 'nafs al-tasawwur' criterion, and identify hypothetical universals as universals without external instances.

Core Idea
A universal is defined by mental sharability, not external reality.

This passage clarifies the definition of universal and particular by adding the crucial qualification 'nafs al-tasawwur' (mere conception). Without this, concepts like the Necessary Existence or hypothetical universals would be misclassified. You will learn to apply this mental criterion correctly.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence: 'if its concept, when obtained in the mind, does not prevent it from being true of many'. This is the basic definition of universal.
2
Understand the qualification
The author stresses that the definition must be understood as 'bare conception' (nafs al-tasawwur). This means we ignore any external facts about the concept's actual instances. He dismisses a variant reading as erroneous.
The concept itself is the meaning; adding 'of its meaning' is redundant.
3
Apply to the Necessary Existence
Consider wajib al-wujud. Externally, only one exists, so sharing is impossible. But if you consider the mere concept, does it prevent sharing? No. So it is universal. If the concept itself blocked sharing, no external proof of oneness would be needed.
4
Examine hypothetical universals
Look at examples like 'nothing', 'impossible', 'non-existent'. These cannot be true of any external thing. But mentally, they can be shared (e.g., the concept of 'nothing' applies to many mental situations). So they are universal.
This shows that a universal does not require actual external individuals.
5
Conclude the principle
The passage ends: 'the individuals of a universal do not necessarily have the universal true of them in actuality'. A universal can have individuals that are purely mental or even impossible.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن مفهومه إذا حصل عند العقل لم يمنع من صدقه على كثيرين. وقد وقع في بعض النسخ «نفس تصور معناه» وهو سهو، وإلا لكان للمعنى معنى، لأن المفهوم هو المعنى. وإنما قيد بـ«نفس التصور» لأن من الكليات ما يمنع الشركة بالنظر إلى الخارج، كواجب الوجود، فإن الشركة فيه ممتنعة بالدليل الخارجي، لكن إذا جرّد العقل النظر إلى مفهومه لم يمتنع من صدقه على كثيرين، فإن مجرد تصوّره لو كان مانعاً من الشركة لم يفتقر في إثبات الوحدانية إلى دليل آخر. وكالكليات الفرضية مثل اللاشيء واللاممكن واللاموجود فإنها يمتنع أن تصدق على شيء من الأشياء في الخارج، لكن لا بالنظر إلى مجرد تصورها. ومن هاهنا يعلم أن أفراد الكلي لا يجب أن يكون الكلي صادقاً عليها في نفس الأمر، بل من أفراده ما يمتنع صدقه عليها في نفس الأمر.
For if its concept, when obtained in the mind, does not prevent it from being true of many. And it occurs in some copies 'the mere conception of its meaning' and that is an oversight, otherwise meaning would have a meaning, because the concept is the meaning. And it was only qualified by 'mere conception' because among universals are those whose sharing is prevented with respect to the external, like the Necessary Existence, for sharing in it is impossible by external proof, but if the mind abstracts to its concept, it is not prevented from being true of many. For if its mere conception were to prevent sharing, then it would not need another proof to establish oneness. And like hypothetical universals such as 'nothing', 'impossible', and 'non-existent', for they are prevented from being true of any external thing, but not with respect to mere conception. And from here it is known that the individuals of a universal do not necessarily have the universal true of them in actuality; rather, among its individuals are those for which it is impossible to be true of them in actuality.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i), expanding on a prior matn. The author first states that a concept is universal if its mere presence in the mind does not prevent it from being predicated of many. He then criticizes a variant reading that says 'mere conception of its meaning' (nafs tasawwur ma'anah) as an error, because the concept itself is the meaning; adding 'of its meaning' would be redundant. The key qualification is 'mere conception' (nafs al-tasawwur). This is necessary because some concepts, like the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud), are such that in external reality it is impossible for them to be shared (i.e., multiple instances), but mentally the mind can still conceive of multiplicity. If mere conception prevented sharing, then one would not need a separate proof (like the proof of tawhid) to show that God is one. So the criterion is mental, not external. Then the author discusses hypothetical universals (kulliyat fardiya) such as 'nothing' (la shay'), 'impossible' (la imkan), and 'non-existent' (la wujud). These concepts cannot be true of any external thing at all, yet the mind can still posit them as shared (e.g., the concept 'nothing' can be applied to many mental fictions). Thus, the universality of a concept is determined solely by whether the mind can posit its sharing, not by whether it actually applies to multiple external individuals. The passage concludes that the individuals of a universal need not actualize the universal in external reality; some universals have individuals that are merely mental or even impossible externally. This guards against the misunderstanding that a universal must have actual instances to be universal.

Key Terms
الكلي
الجزئي
نفس التصور
الكلية الفرضية

Key Takeaway
A concept is universal if its bare conception does not prevent mental sharing; external actuality is irrelevant.

Defining Universal and Particular: The Role of Mere Conception
Commentator explains the necessity of 'mere conception' in defining universal and particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition of 'mere conception' in the definitions of universal and particular, and explain why it is necessary to exclude hypothetical universals from being misclassified as particulars.

Core Idea
Mere conception is the gate that keeps impossible universals from being mistaken for particulars.

In Islamic logic, the definitions of universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i) hinge on a subtle but crucial condition: whether the mere act of conceiving the meaning prevents predication of many. Without this condition, certain universals that are conceptually impossible to share would wrongly be considered particulars. This lesson unpacks that condition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitions
Find where the commentator defines al-kulli and al-juz'i in the passage. Notice the phrase 'بمجرد تصوره' (by mere conception).
The definitions are not given as single sentences but are implied through the condition.
2
Identify the condition
The condition is that the definition considers 'mere conception' itself (nafs al-tasawwur). Why is this added? To exclude universals whose conceiving prevents sharing, like 'necessary existence' in some interpretations.
The passage says: 'if mere conception were not considered... those universals would enter the definition of particular.'
3
Apply the condition
Test a hypothetical universal like 'necessary existence' (wajib al-wujud). Can it be predicated of many by mere conception? No. So without the condition, it would be considered particular—an error.
This shows the condition ensures the definition is both comprehensive and exclusive.
4
Summarize the purpose
The condition ensures the definition of universal is comprehensive (jami') for all true universals and the definition of particular is exclusive (mani') against universals that mistakenly appear particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليها، بل من أفراده ما يمتنع أن يصدق الكلي عليه في الخارج، إذا لم يمنع العقل من صدقه عليه بمجرد تصوّره، فلو لم يعتبر «نفس التصوّر» في تعريف الكلّي و الجزئي لدخل تلك الكليات في تعريف الجزئي - فلايكون مانعاً - وخرجت عن تعريف الكلي - فلا يكون جامعاً. و بيان التسمية بالكلّي و الجزئي أنّ الكلّي جزء للجزئي غالباً كالإنسان - فإنّه جزء لزيد - والحيوان - فإنّه جزء للإنسان - والجسم - فإنه جزء للحيوان - فيكون الجزئي كلاً، والكلي جزء له، وكلية الشيء إنما تكون بالنسبة إلى الجزئي، فيكون ذلك الشيء منسوباً إلى الكل، من واحد ، و الكليات الفرضيّة يمتنع صدقها في نفس الأمر على شيء واحد كليته منه. - فضلاً عما هو أكثر . فالمعتبر في أفراد الكلّي إمكان فرض صدقه عليها، إذ بهذا المقدار تتحقق وكون تلك الأفراد له محققة في نفس الأمر غير لازم لكليته. نعم ما كان فرداً للكلّي في نفس الأمر فلابد أن يصدق عليه ذلك الكلّي في نفس الأمر، أو أمكن صدقه عليه فيها. وستظهر فائدة هذه النكتة التي علمت هاهنا من قوله في مباحث تحقيق مفهومات القضايا المحصورة (شريف). (1) متعلّق بقوله : «لأنّ من الكليات مايمنع الشركة... الخ (شريف). (۲) إشارة إلى أن بعض الكليات ليس جزء الجزئياته كالخاصة والعرض العام، وأما الثلاثة الباقية، فهي أجزاء الجزئياتها، فإن الجنس والفصل جزءان لماهية النوع، والنوع جزء للشخص من حيث هو شخص - وإن كان تمام ماهيته (شريف). (۳) لا يخفى أن هذا المعنى إنما يظهر في الكلّي بالقياس إلى الجزئي الإضافي، فإن كل واحد منهما متضايف للآخر، إذ معنى الجزئي الإضافي هو «المندرج تحت الكليات الخمس». والمنسوب إلى الكلّ كلّي، وكذلك جزئية الشيء إنما هي بالنسبة إلى الكلي، فيكون منسوباً إلى الجزء، والمنسوب إلى الجزء جزئي. واعلم أن الكلية والجزئية إنما تعتبران بالذات في المعاني، وأما في الألفاظ فقد تسمّى كلّيّة وجزئية بالعرض، تسمية الدالّ باسم المدلول.
Upon them, but some of its individuals are such that it is impossible for the universal to be true of them externally, if the intellect does not prevent its being true of them by mere conception. So if 'mere conception' were not considered in the definition of universal and particular, those universals would enter into the definition of particular—so it would not be a preventer—and they would exit from the definition of universal—so it would not be comprehensive. And the explanation of the naming 'universal' and 'particular' is that the universal is a part of the particular often, like 'human'—for it is a part of Zayd—and 'animal'—for it is a part of human—and 'body'—for it is a part of animal. So the particular is a whole, and the universal is a part of it. The universality of a thing is only in relation to the particular, so that thing is attributed to the whole. And hypothetical universals cannot be true in actuality of a single thing, let alone more. So what is considered in the individuals of the universal is the possibility of supposing it true of them, for by this measure the existence of those individuals for it is actualized; their being actual for it is not necessary for its universality. Yes, whatever is an individual of the universal in actuality must have that universal true of it in actuality, or it must be possible for it to be true of it therein. The benefit of this point that you have learned here will appear from his statement in the investigations of the concepts of quantified propositions (al-Sharif). (1) It relates to his statement: 'Because some universals prevent sharing... etc.' (al-Sharif). (2) It indicates that some universals are not parts of their particulars, like the specific difference and the general accident. As for the remaining three, they are parts of their particulars. For genus and differentia are two parts of the essence of the species, and species is a part of the individual insofar as it is an individual—even if it is the entirety of its essence (al-Sharif). (3) It is not hidden that this meaning appears only in the universal in relation to the relative particular, for each of them is correlative to the other. For the meaning of the relative particular is 'that which falls under the five universals.' And what is attributed to the whole is universal. Likewise, the particularity of a thing is only in relation to the universal, so it is attributed to the part, and what is attributed to the part is particular. And know that universality and particularity are considered essentially in meanings. As for expressions, they may be called universal and particular accidentally, naming the signifier by the name of the signified.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on the Shamsiyya, expanding on the definitions of universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). The commentator begins by clarifying a condition: the definition of universal must consider 'mere conception' (nafs al-tasawwur) to exclude impossible universals that cannot be predicated of any individual. Without this condition, some universals that are conceptually impossible to share (like 'necessary existence' in some interpretations) would wrongly be considered particulars. The passage then explains the etymology: a universal is called 'kulli' because it is often a part of its particulars. For example, 'human' is part of Zayd, 'animal' is part of human, etc. Thus the particular is the whole, and the universal is its part. However, this part-whole relation is not always the case; some universals (like specific difference and general accident) are not parts of their particulars. A key point is that universality is relative to the particular: a thing is universal by being related to the whole (the particular). The commentator also distinguishes between actual individuals and hypothetical ones: a universal need not have actual individuals; it is enough that it can be supposed to apply to them. The text then discusses relative versus absolute universality/particularity. Relative universality and particularity are correlative (like fatherhood and sonship). Absolute particularity (al-juz'iyya al-haqiqiyya) is the opposite of universality in the sense of privation: particularity is the prevention of sharing (being predicated of many), while universality is the absence of such prevention. The passage ends with a note that in expressions, we sometimes call words universal or particular accidentally, i.e., by the name of their meanings.

الكلي
الجزئي
نفس التصور

B) Hypothetical universals would enter the definition of particular.

Key Takeaway
The condition of 'mere conception' is essential to maintain the logical distinction between universal and particular.

The Naming: Why Universal is Called Part of Particular
Commentator explains the etymological relation between universal and particular as part-whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a universal is called a 'part' of its particular, and distinguish between relative and absolute particularity.

Core Idea
A universal is called a 'part' because it often enters into the constitution of its particular.

Why is the term 'universal' (kulli) related to 'whole' (kull)? The commentator explains that a universal is often a constituent part of its particular, like 'human' is part of Zayd. This section of the passage reveals the logical relationship between universal and particular and introduces the concept of relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi).

Guided Path
1
Find the part-whole examples
The commentator gives examples: human is part of Zayd, animal is part of human, body is part of animal. Locate these in the passage.
These illustrate that universals are often components of their particulars.
2
Understand the relation
The universal is part of the particular; the particular is the whole. So the particular is called 'whole' (kull) and the universal is attributed to it as 'kulli' (universal).
This is the etymological reason for the term 'kulli'.
3
Identify exceptions
Not all universals are parts of their particulars. The passage mentions specific difference (fasl) and general accident ('arad 'amm) as exceptions. They are true predicates but are not constituents of the essence.
This nuance prevents overgeneralization.
4
Distinguish relative particular
Relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi) is that which falls under a universal and is correlative to it. For example, Zayd is a relative particular to 'human'. This contrasts with absolute particular which prevents predication of many by its essence.
The passage notes that universality and particularity are correlative in the relative sense.
5
Apply the distinction
Test examples: 'human' is a universal relative to Zayd; Zayd is a relative particular. As an absolute particular, 'Zayd' cannot be predicated of many. So the same concept can be universal in one relation and particular in another.

(۳) لا يخفى أن هذا المعنى إنما يظهر في الكلّي بالقياس إلى الجزئي الإضافي، فإن كل واحد منهما متضايف للآخر، إذ معنى الجزئي الإضافي هو «المندرج تحت الكليات الخمس». والمنسوب إلى الكلّ كلّي، وكذلك جزئية الشيء إنما هي بالنسبة إلى الكلي، فيكون منسوباً إلى الجزء، والمنسوب إلى الجزء جزئي. واعلم أن الكلية والجزئية إنما تعتبران بالذات في المعاني، وأما في الألفاظ فقد تسمّى كلّيّة وجزئية بالعرض، تسمية الدالّ باسم المدلول.
Upon them, but some of its individuals are such that it is impossible for the universal to be true of them externally, if the intellect does not prevent its being true of them by mere conception. So if 'mere conception' were not considered in the definition of universal and particular, those universals would enter into the definition of particular—so it would not be a preventer—and they would exit from the definition of universal—so it would not be comprehensive. And the explanation of the naming 'universal' and 'particular' is that the universal is a part of the particular often, like 'human'—for it is a part of Zayd—and 'animal'—for it is a part of human—and 'body'—for it is a part of animal. So the particular is a whole, and the universal is a part of it. The universality of a thing is only in relation to the particular, so that thing is attributed to the whole. And hypothetical universals cannot be true in actuality of a single thing, let alone more. So what is considered in the individuals of the universal is the possibility of supposing it true of them, for by this measure the existence of those individuals for it is actualized; their being actual for it is not necessary for its universality. Yes, whatever is an individual of the universal in actuality must have that universal true of it in actuality, or it must be possible for it to be true of it therein. The benefit of this point that you have learned here will appear from his statement in the investigations of the concepts of quantified propositions (al-Sharif). (1) It relates to his statement: 'Because some universals prevent sharing... etc.' (al-Sharif). (2) It indicates that some universals are not parts of their particulars, like the specific difference and the general accident. As for the remaining three, they are parts of their particulars. For genus and differentia are two parts of the essence of the species, and species is a part of the individual insofar as it is an individual—even if it is the entirety of its essence (al-Sharif). (3) It is not hidden that this meaning appears only in the universal in relation to the relative particular, for each of them is correlative to the other. For the meaning of the relative particular is 'that which falls under the five universals.' And what is attributed to the whole is universal. Likewise, the particularity of a thing is only in relation to the universal, so it is attributed to the part, and what is attributed to the part is particular. And know that universality and particularity are considered essentially in meanings. As for expressions, they may be called universal and particular accidentally, naming the signifier by the name of the signified.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on the Shamsiyya, expanding on the definitions of universal (kulli) and particular (juz'i). The commentator begins by clarifying a condition: the definition of universal must consider 'mere conception' (nafs al-tasawwur) to exclude impossible universals that cannot be predicated of any individual. Without this condition, some universals that are conceptually impossible to share (like 'necessary existence' in some interpretations) would wrongly be considered particulars. The passage then explains the etymology: a universal is called 'kulli' because it is often a part of its particulars. For example, 'human' is part of Zayd, 'animal' is part of human, etc. Thus the particular is the whole, and the universal is its part. However, this part-whole relation is not always the case; some universals (like specific difference and general accident) are not parts of their particulars. A key point is that universality is relative to the particular: a thing is universal by being related to the whole (the particular). The commentator also distinguishes between actual individuals and hypothetical ones: a universal need not have actual individuals; it is enough that it can be supposed to apply to them. The text then discusses relative versus absolute universality/particularity. Relative universality and particularity are correlative (like fatherhood and sonship). Absolute particularity (al-juz'iyya al-haqiqiyya) is the opposite of universality in the sense of privation: particularity is the prevention of sharing (being predicated of many), while universality is the absence of such prevention. The passage ends with a note that in expressions, we sometimes call words universal or particular accidentally, i.e., by the name of their meanings.

Key Terms
الجزئي الإضافي
الكلية
الجزئية

Key Takeaway
A universal is called a part of its particular, but this part-whole relation is not absolute; some universals are not constituents of their particulars.

Definition and Conditions of the Differentia (Fasl)
Author defines fasl and refutes an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that a differentia must satisfy to be a valid answer to 'ayy shay' huwa fi jawharihi?' and distinguish it from genus and property.

Core Idea
The differentia is an essential distinguisher that must not be the entire commonality between the thing and another species.

In this passage, the author defines the differentia (fasl) as a universal essential predicate that answers 'which thing is it in its essence?' We will unpack the definition, examine the conditions, and see how it differs from genus (jins) and property (khassa). An objection about the scope of distinction is also addressed, refining the definition further.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined: fasl (differentia). The author states that when a substantial distinguisher is sought, the answer is the differentia.
2
Extract the genus and differentia in the definition
The author defines fasl as 'kulli dhati' (universal essential) that is predicated in answer to 'ayy shay' huwa?', not 'ma huwa?'. This excludes species, genus, and common accident because they answer 'what is it?' or are not predicative answers.
The genus in the definition is 'universal essential'; the differentia is the specific phrasing of the question.
3
Examine the condition 'in its essence'
The clause 'fi jawharihi' (in its essence) excludes the property (khassa), which distinguishes accidentally, not essentially.
This ensures only essential distinguishers qualify.
4
Analyze the objection and reply
If the questioner seeks a distinguisher in general (even from some others), genus would also distinguish. The author replies that mere distinguishing in general is insufficient; the distinguisher must not be the entire commonality between the thing and another species.
This prevents genus from answering the question.
5
Test the definition with examples
Apply the definition to examples: for 'which substance?', all differentiae answer; for 'which body?', 'possessing dimensions' is invalid because it is common; for 'which animal?', only 'rational' is valid.
The examples show how the condition works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يميزه يصلح للجواب ؛ ثم إن طلب المميز الجوهري يكون الجواب بالفصل، وإن طلب المميّز العرضي يكون الجواب بالخاصة. فـ«الكلي» جنس يشمل سائر الكليات، ويقولنا «يحمل على الشيء في جواب أي شيء هو يخرج النوع والجنس والعرض العام، لأن النوع والجنس يقالان في جواب «ماهو ؟ لا في جواب «أي شيء هو ؟» والعرض العام لا يقال في الجواب أصلاً. ويقولنا في جوهره يخرج الخاصة لأنها وإن كانت مميزة للشيء، لكن لا في جوهره وذاته، بل في عرضه. فإن قلت : السائل بـ«أي شيء هو ؟ » إن طلب مميز الشيء عن جميع الأغيار، لا يكون مثل «الحساس» فصلاً للإنسان - لأنه لا يميزه عن جميع - وإن طلب المميز في الجملة - سواء كان عن جميع الأغيار أو عن بعضها - فالجنس مميز للشيء عن بعضها، فيجب أن يكون صالحاً للجواب، فلا يخرج عن الحد. وقابل الأبعاد - وأن يجاب بالخاصة أيضاً. وإذا قيل : «أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟ » لم يصح الجواب بالخاصة، وصح بالفصول المذكورة كلها، وكذا إذا قيل : «أي جوهر هو في ذاته ؟ » صح الجواب بجميع تلك الفصول. وأما إذا قيل : «أي جسم هو في ذاته ؟» لم يصح الجواب إلا بما عدا «القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة»، وإذا قيل : أي جسم نام هو في ذاته ؟» لم يصح الجواب بـ «القابل للأبعاد» و «النامي» أيضاً، وإذا قيل : «أي حيوان هو في ذاته ؟» تعين «الناطق» للجواب (شريف). فنقول : لا يكتفى في جواب أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟» بالتمييز في الجملة، بل لابد معه أن لا يكون تمام المشترك بين الشيء ونوع آخر، فالجنس خارج عن التعريف ؛ ولما كان محصله أن الفصل كلّي ذاتي لا يكون مقولاً في جواب «ما هو ؟» ويكون مميزاً للشيء في الجملة، فلو فرضنا ماهيّة مركبة من أمرين متساويين أو أمور متساوية كماهية الجنس العالي والفصل الأخير - كالناطق – كان كل منهما فصلاً لها، لأنه يميز الماهية تمييزاً جوهرياً عما يشاركها في الوجود ويحمل عليها في جواب «أي موجود هو ؟». واعلم أن قدماء المنطقيين زعموا أن كل ماهية لها فصل وجب أن يكون لها جنس، حتّى أنّ الشيخ تبعهم في الشفاء" وحد الفصل بأنه كلّي مقول على الشيء في جواب أي شيء هو في جوهره من جنسه»، وإذ لم يساعده البرهان على ذلك تبه المصنف على ضعفه بالمشاركة في الوجود أولاً، وبإيراد هذا الاحتمال ثانياً.
What distinguishes it is fit for the answer; then if a substantial distinguisher is sought, the answer is with the differentia, and if an accidental distinguisher is sought, the answer is with the property. So 'universal' is a genus that includes all universals, and our saying 'predicated of the thing in answer to "which thing is it?" excludes species, genus, and common accident, because species and genus are said in answer to 'what is it?', not in answer to 'which thing is it?', and common accident is not said in the answer at all. And our saying 'in its essence' excludes the property because although it distinguishes the thing, it does so not in its essence and substance, but in its accident. If it is said: the questioner by 'which thing is it?' – if he seeks a distinguisher of the thing from all others, then 'sensitive' would not be a differentia for man because it does not distinguish him from all; but if he seeks a distinguisher in general – whether from all others or from some – then the genus distinguishes the thing from some of them, so it must be fit for the answer, and thus not excluded from the definition. And [the example of] 'possessing dimensions' – and that the property may also answer. And if it is said: 'which thing is it in its essence?', the answer by the property is not valid, but it is valid by all the mentioned differentiae; similarly if it is said: 'which substance is it in itself?', the answer is valid by all those differentiae. And if it is said: 'which body is it in itself?', the answer is valid only by what excludes 'possessing three dimensions'; and if it is said: 'which growing body is it in itself?', the answer is not valid by 'possessing dimensions' and 'growing' either; and if it is said: 'which animal is it in itself?', 'rational' is specified for the answer (al-Sharif). So we say: in the answer to 'which thing is it in its essence?', mere distinguishing in general is not sufficient; rather, it is necessary along with it that it not be the entire commonality between the thing and another species, so the genus is excluded from the definition. And since the upshot is that the differentia is a universal essential not said in answer to 'what is it?' and is a distinguisher of the thing in general, if we suppose an essence composed of two co-equal matters or co-equal matters like the essence of the highest genus and the ultimate differentia – like 'rational' – then each of them would be a differentia for it, because it distinguishes the essence with an essential distinction from what shares existence with it and is predicated of it in answer to 'which existent is it?'. And know that the ancient logicians claimed that every essence having a differentia must have a genus, such that even al-Shaykh followed them in al-Shifa' and defined the differentia as 'a universal predicated of the thing in answer to "which thing is it in its essence" from its genus'; and since proof did not support him on that, the author pointed out its weakness by [the example of] sharing in existence first, and by presenting this possibility second.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage defines the differentia (fasl) and its conditions for being a proper answer to the question 'ayy shay' huwa?'. It begins by stating that if a substantial distinguisher is sought, the answer is the differentia; if accidental, the answer is the property (khassa). The author then explains the definition: 'universal' is the genus covering all universals; the clause 'predicated in answer to "which thing is it?"' excludes species, genus, and common accident because those answer 'what is it?' or are not predicative answers at all. The clause 'in its essence' excludes property because property distinguishes accidentally, not essentially. An objection is raised: if the questioner seeks a distinguisher in general (even from some others), then genus would also distinguish and should be allowed. The author replies by distinguishing: the answer to 'which thing is it in its essence?' requires that the distinguisher not be the entire commonality between the thing and another species; genus is too broad and does not distinguish essentially. He then gives examples: for 'which substance?', all differentiae answer; for 'which body?', the differentia cannot be 'possessing dimensions' because that is common; for 'which growing body?', both 'possessing dimensions' and 'growing' are excluded; for 'which animal?', only 'rational' is valid. The passage then discusses a hypothetical essence composed of two co-equal components (like highest genus and ultimate differentia), where each serves as differentia. Finally, the author critiques ancient logicians (including Ibn Sina) who insisted that every essence with a differentia must have a genus, providing a counterexample: an essence merely participating in existence without a genus. The author shows the weakness of their view by this and by the possibility of co-equal components.

What Is Mantiq?

Critique of Ancient Logicians on the Necessity of Genus
Author critiques ancient logicians on genus necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the ancient logicians' claim that every essence with a differentia must have a genus, and evaluate the author's counterexamples that challenge this claim.

Core Idea
An essence can have a differentia without a genus, as shown by the example of mere participation in existence and co‑equal components.

The author now turns to critique the view of ancient logicians, including Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh), who insisted that any essence with a differentia necessarily has a genus. He presents two counterexamples: an essence that only shares in existence (without a genus), and an essence composed of two co‑equal parts where each can serve as a differentia. This lesson examines the force of these objections.

Guided Path
1
State the target doctrine
The ancient logicians claimed: 'Kull mahiyya laha fasl wajab an yakun laha jins' – every essence having a differentia must have a genus. This was upheld by Ibn Sina in al-Shifa'.
The claim is about the necessary composition of essence.
2
Identify the first counterexample
The author points to 'al-musharaka fi al-wujud' – an essence that merely participates in existence without a genus. If such an essence exists, it has a differentia (e.g., its unique property) but no genus. This shows the claim is not universally true.
This relies on the notion of a simple essence.
3
Identify the second counterexample
The author supposes a mahiyya composed of two co‑equal matters (e.g., the highest genus and the ultimate differentia, like 'rational' and 'animal' in a simple essence). In that case, each component can serve as a differentia for the whole, yet there is no genus above them.
This challenges the idea that a genus must precede the differentia.
4
Evaluate the author's argument
Both counterexamples are logically possible. The first shows that existence alone can be the commonality, not a genus. The second shows that two co‑equal differentiae can coexist without a higher genus. The author concludes that the ancient claim is weak ('da'f').
The critique targets the necessity, not the possibility, of genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يميزه يصلح للجواب ؛ ثم إن طلب المميز الجوهري يكون الجواب بالفصل، وإن طلب المميّز العرضي يكون الجواب بالخاصة. فـ«الكلي» جنس يشمل سائر الكليات، ويقولنا «يحمل على الشيء في جواب أي شيء هو يخرج النوع والجنس والعرض العام، لأن النوع والجنس يقالان في جواب «ماهو ؟ لا في جواب «أي شيء هو ؟» والعرض العام لا يقال في الجواب أصلاً. ويقولنا في جوهره يخرج الخاصة لأنها وإن كانت مميزة للشيء، لكن لا في جوهره وذاته، بل في عرضه. فإن قلت : السائل بـ«أي شيء هو ؟ » إن طلب مميز الشيء عن جميع الأغيار، لا يكون مثل «الحساس» فصلاً للإنسان - لأنه لا يميزه عن جميع - وإن طلب المميز في الجملة - سواء كان عن جميع الأغيار أو عن بعضها - فالجنس مميز للشيء عن بعضها، فيجب أن يكون صالحاً للجواب، فلا يخرج عن الحد. وقابل الأبعاد - وأن يجاب بالخاصة أيضاً. وإذا قيل : «أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟ » لم يصح الجواب بالخاصة، وصح بالفصول المذكورة كلها، وكذا إذا قيل : «أي جوهر هو في ذاته ؟ » صح الجواب بجميع تلك الفصول. وأما إذا قيل : «أي جسم هو في ذاته ؟» لم يصح الجواب إلا بما عدا «القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة»، وإذا قيل : أي جسم نام هو في ذاته ؟» لم يصح الجواب بـ «القابل للأبعاد» و «النامي» أيضاً، وإذا قيل : «أي حيوان هو في ذاته ؟» تعين «الناطق» للجواب (شريف). فنقول : لا يكتفى في جواب أي شيء هو في جوهره ؟» بالتمييز في الجملة، بل لابد معه أن لا يكون تمام المشترك بين الشيء ونوع آخر، فالجنس خارج عن التعريف ؛ ولما كان محصله أن الفصل كلّي ذاتي لا يكون مقولاً في جواب «ما هو ؟» ويكون مميزاً للشيء في الجملة، فلو فرضنا ماهيّة مركبة من أمرين متساويين أو أمور متساوية كماهية الجنس العالي والفصل الأخير - كالناطق – كان كل منهما فصلاً لها، لأنه يميز الماهية تمييزاً جوهرياً عما يشاركها في الوجود ويحمل عليها في جواب «أي موجود هو ؟». واعلم أن قدماء المنطقيين زعموا أن كل ماهية لها فصل وجب أن يكون لها جنس، حتّى أنّ الشيخ تبعهم في الشفاء" وحد الفصل بأنه كلّي مقول على الشيء في جواب أي شيء هو في جوهره من جنسه»، وإذ لم يساعده البرهان على ذلك تبه المصنف على ضعفه بالمشاركة في الوجود أولاً، وبإيراد هذا الاحتمال ثانياً.

Checkpoint
What is the author's first objection to the ancient logicians' claim that every essence with a differentia has a genus?
A) The example of sharing in existence without a genus
B) The example of co-equal components
C) The claim that differentia can be accidental
D) The idea that genus is always broader than differentia

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the necessary connection between differentia and genus by showing that an essence can have a differentia without a genus, as with mere participation in existence or co-equal composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Explain why the example of 'sharing in existence' undermines the claim that every differentia implies a genus. Then, provide another example from Islamic thought (e.g., angels) where a species might have a differentia but no clear genus.
?
In a debate, a student argues: 'Since humans are rational animals, rationality is a differentia that implies a genus (animal).' Using the author's critique, show that this inference is not always necessary.

Proximate and Remote Differentia
Author defines proximate and remote differentia with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish proximate from remote differentia and apply this distinction to definitions in Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The proximity of a differentia depends on the level of genus it distinguishes.

Definitions in logic and Islamic sciences rely on identifying the differentia that sets a species apart from others sharing its genus. This lesson clarifies the difference between proximate and remote differentia based on the level of genus they address, helping you recognize the hierarchical structure in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the term 'differentia' (fasl) in the Arabic. The passage defines two types: proximate and remote.
The differentia is what distinguishes a species from other species in the same genus.
2
Extract the condition for proximate differentia
The proximate differentia distinguishes the species from others that share the proximate (immediate) genus. For example, 'rational' is the proximate differentia for 'human' because it separates humans from other animals sharing the genus 'animal'.
The proximate genus is the closest genus above the species.
3
Extract the condition for remote differentia
The remote differentia distinguishes from shares in the remote (higher) genus. For instance, 'sentient' might be a remote differentia for 'human' if we consider the genus 'living being'.
The remote genus is a more general genus higher up the hierarchy.
4
Understand the special cases: highest genus and ultimate differentia
The marginal note explains that the highest genus (jins 'ali) and ultimate differentia (fasl akhir) cannot be composed of genus and differentia; otherwise they would not be the highest or ultimate. This preserves the hierarchical structure.
If the highest genus had a genus above it, it would not be the highest.
5
Apply to an Islamic definition
Take the definition of 'wudu' (ablution) as 'washing the limbs with water with intention'. The proximate genus is 'washing' and the differentia might be 'with water and intention'. If we consider a broader genus like 'ritual purification', the differentia would be different.
This shows how the same species can have different differentia depending on the genus level chosen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢٣- الفصل القريب والبعيد] قال : والفصل المميز للنوع عن مشاركه في الجنس «قريب» إن ميزه عن مشاركه في الجنس القريب، و«بعيد» إن ميزه عن مشاركه في الجنس البعيد. (وإنما مثل بهما لامتناع تركيبهما من الجنس والفصل معاً، وإلا لم يكن الجنس العالي جنساً عالياً، ولا الفصل الأخير فصلاً أخيراً، أما إذا فرض تركيبهما من أجزاء وجب أن تكون تلك الأجزاء متساوية (شريف).
23 – Proximate and Remote Differentia. He said: And the differentia that distinguishes the species from its shares in the genus is 'proximate' if it distinguishes it from its shares in the proximate genus, and 'remote' if it distinguishes it from its shares in the remote genus. (He only gave these two examples because it is impossible for them to be composed of genus and differentia together; otherwise the highest genus would not be a highest genus, nor the ultimate differentia an ultimate differentia; but if they are supposed to be composed of parts, then those parts must be equal (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of differentia based on the level of genus they apply to. The author states that the differentia that distinguishes a species from other species sharing its genus is called 'proximate' (qarib) if it distinguishes from those sharing the proximate genus (i.e., the immediate genus), and 'remote' (ba'id) if it distinguishes from those sharing the remote genus (i.e., a higher, more general genus). The marginal note explains that these two examples (proximate and remote) are given because it is impossible for the highest genus and ultimate differentia themselves to be composed of genus and differentia; otherwise the highest genus would have a genus above it, and the ultimate differentia would have a more specific differentia. If they were composed of parts, those parts would have to be co-equal. This highlights the special status of the highest genus (which has no genus) and the ultimate differentia (which is the final differentia in a hierarchy).

Key Takeaway
A differentia is called proximate or remote based on the level of genus it distinguishes within the logical hierarchy.

The Differentia's Dual Role: Constitutive of Species, Divisive of Genus
Author defines dual relationship of differentia to species and genus with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the dual role of the differentia as constitutive (muqawwim) of species and divisive (muqassim) of genus, and apply this distinction to Islamic definitional examples.

Core Idea
The differentia builds the species and carves the genus.

In this passage, the author explains how one term—the differentia (فصل)—plays two distinct roles depending on what it relates to. When attached to the species, it is constitutive (مقوم), forming part of its essence. When attached to the genus, it is divisive (مقسم), splitting the genus into its species. This dual nature is essential for constructing correct definitions (حدود) in logic and subsequently in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations
The passage explicitly states two relationships: (1) the differentia's relation to the species (نوع) as constitutive, and (2) its relation to the genus (جنس) as divisive. Look for the phrases 'نسبته إلى النوع' and 'نسبته إلى الجنس'.
The same differentia performs both functions depending on perspective.
2
Understand 'constitutive' (muqawwim)
When the differentia is added to a species, it is 'inside its constitution' (داخل في قوامه) and is a part (جزء) of it. For example, 'rational' (ناطق) is part of the essence of 'human' (إنسان).
Constitutive means the differentia is an essential part of the definition of the species.
3
Understand 'divisive' (muqassim)
When the differentia is added to the genus, it 'obtains a division' (محصل قسم) of the genus. The composite of genus + differentia forms a species that is a subcategory of the genus. For example, 'rational' added to 'animal' yields 'rational animal', a species of animal.
Divisive does not mean the differentia alone divides; it divides only when combined with the genus.
4
Apply the example of 'rational'
The author uses 'rational' (ناطق) to illustrate: when related to 'human' (إنسان), it is internal to its constitution; when related to 'animal' (حيوان), it forms the species 'rational animal' (حيوان ناطق) which is a division of animal.
This example shows the two different functions of one differentia clearly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما نسبته إلى النوع : فبأنه مقوم له - أي داخل في قوامه وجزء له. وأما نسبته إلى الجنس : فإنه مقسم له - أي محصل قسم له - فإنّه إذا انضم إلى الجنس صار المجموع قسماً من الجنس ونوعاً له، مثلاً «الناطق» إذا نسب إلى «الإنسان فهو داخل في قوامه وماهيته، وإذا نسب إلى «الحيوان» صار حيواناً ناطقاً، وهو قسم من الحيوان.
As for its relationship to the species: it is constitutive of it—i.e., internal to its constitution and a part of it. And as for its relationship to the genus: it is divisive of it—i.e., it obtains a division of it—for when it joins the genus, the composite becomes a division of the genus and a species of it. For example, 'rational' when related to 'human' is internal to its constitution and essence, and when related to 'animal' it becomes a rational animal, and it is a division of animal.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines the dual relationship of the differentia (فصل) to the species (نوع) and genus (جنس). The differentia is constitutive (مقوم) of the species, meaning it is an essential part of its quiddity, and it is divisive (مقسم) of the genus, meaning it divides the genus into its species by combining with it. The author gives the example of 'rational' (ناطق): when attributed to 'human', it is internal to its constitution; when attributed to 'animal', it yields 'rational animal', which is a species of animal. This illustrates the dual role of the differentia.

Key Takeaway
The differentia serves two functions: it constitutes the species as an essential part, and it divides the genus by forming a species when combined with it.

The Definition and Primary Division of Proposition
Author defines proposition and divides it into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given declarative statement qualifies as a logical proposition and classify it as categorical or conditional.

Core Idea
A proposition is a truth-apt statement; its primary division is into categorical and conditional.

This passage introduces the foundational unit of logic—the proposition. Before constructing arguments, we must know what a proposition is and its basic types. The Qutbi defines proposition as a statement that can be true or false, then splits it into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). Understanding this division is crucial because every syllogism is built from propositions.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of proposition
The definition is: 'a statement of which it can be said that its utterer is truthful or lying'. This means a proposition must be declarative and truth-apt. Questions, commands, and wishes are not propositions.
This definition excludes non-declarative sentences.
2
Identify the two parts of a categorical proposition
A categorical proposition can be resolved into two singular terms: a subject (mawdu') and a predicate (mahmul). Example: 'Zayd is knowledgeable' has subject 'Zayd' and predicate 'knowledgeable'.
The subject and predicate are usually nouns or noun phrases.
3
Distinguish conditional from categorical
If a statement cannot be resolved into two singular terms, it is conditional. Conditionals often have an 'if...then' or 'either...or' structure. Example: 'If the sun rises, it is day' cannot be broken into subject-predicate.
Conditionals may be conjunctive or disjunctive; the text mentions implicative and coincidental subtypes.
4
Apply the primary division to new examples
Given a set of statements, decide if each is a proposition (truth-apt) and then whether it is categorical or conditional. Practice with examples from Islamic sciences.
This step solidifies the classification skill.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية وأقسامها
«القضية» قول يصح أن يقال لقائله: «إنّه صادق فيه» أو «كاذب». وهي «حملية» إن انحلت بطرفيها إلى مفردين، كقولك: «زيد عالم» «زيد ليس بعالم»، وشرطية إن لم تنحل.
القول : لما فرغ من مباحث القول الشارح، شرع في بيان مباحث الحجة، ولما توقف معرفتها على معرفة القضايا وأحكامها وضع المقالة الثانية لبيان ذلك، ورتبها على مقدمة وثلاثة فصول.
أما المقدمة : ففي تعريف القضية وأقسامها الأولية - أي الحاصلة بحسب القسمة الأولى، فإن القضية تنقسم أولا إلى الحملية والشرطية، ثم الحملية تنقسم إلى ضرورية واللاضرورية - مثلاً - والشرطية إلى لزومية واتفاقية؛ فأقسام الحمليّة والشرطية هي أقسام القضية إلا أنها
The proposition and its divisions. The proposition is a statement which can be said of its utterer: 'He is truthful in it' or 'lying'. It is 'categorical' if it can be resolved by its two parts into two singular terms, like your saying: 'Zayd is knowledgeable', 'Zayd is not knowledgeable', and 'conditional' if it cannot be resolved. The commentary: When he finished from the discussions of the explicatory statement, he began to explain the discussions of the argument. And since knowledge of it depends on knowledge of propositions and their rulings, he placed the second article to explain that, and arranged it on an introduction and three chapters. As for the introduction: it is about the definition of the proposition and its primary divisions - i.e., those obtained according to the first division, for the proposition divides first into categorical and conditional, then the categorical divides into necessary and non-necessary - for example - and the conditional into implicative and coincidental; so the divisions of categorical and conditional are the divisions of the proposition, except that...
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage opens the second article of the Qutbi, which deals with propositions and their rulings. The author first defines the proposition (qadiyya) as a statement that can be truthfully or falsely attributed to its utterer. This definition captures the essence of a logical proposition: it is a declarative statement capable of truth-value. He then provides its primary division: if the proposition can be resolved into two simple terms (subject and predicate), it is categorical (hamliyya); otherwise, it is conditional (shartiyya). Examples: 'Zayd is knowledgeable' and 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' are categorical because they have a subject ('Zayd') and a predicate ('knowledgeable'). Conditional propositions, like 'If the sun rises, it is day', cannot be resolved into two singular terms. The commentary further explains that the author first completed the section on the explicatory statement (al-qawl al-sharih), i.e., the five universals, and now moves to the argument (al-hujja). Since arguments are composed of propositions, he introduces propositions first. The primary division is the first cut: categorical vs. conditional. Subsequent divisions (necessary/non-necessary for categorical, implicative/coincidental for conditional) are secondary. This passage thus establishes the foundational classification of logical propositions.

Key Terms
القضية
الحملية
الشرطية

Distinguishing Categorical from Conditional Propositions
Author critiques and refines the division of propositions into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from a conditional proposition using the refined criterion of whether the parts contain a complete relation considered in detail.

Core Idea
The distinction hinges on whether the proposition's parts contain a complete relation considered in detail.

The traditional definition of categorical and conditional propositions uses the simplicity of subject and predicate terms. But this leaves some cases unclear, like 'Zayd, his father is standing'—a categorical proposition with a propositional predicate. The author presents and refines a more precise criterion: whether the proposition resolves into two propositions, or more fundamentally, whether its parts contain a complete relation (nisba tāmma) considered in detail. Mastering this refinement is essential for correctly classifying propositions in advanced logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Check if both parts are single terms
If the subject and predicate are each a single term (e.g., 'Man is animal'), the proposition is categorical. If not, proceed.
This matches the initial definition, but it fails for propositions like 'Zayd, his father is standing'.
2
Identify any relations in the parts
A relation can be a simple attribute (restrictive) or a complete judgment (predicative). Restrictive relations keep the proposition categorical; complete relations may make it conditional.
Example of restrictive: 'rational animal' in 'Rational animal is a laughing body'.
3
Classify based on completeness and detail
If a complete relation appears in one part only, or in both but considered summarily, the proposition is still categorical. Only when both parts contain a complete relation considered in detail is it conditional.
Summary consideration means the relation is taken as a whole, not analyzed into subject, predicate, and judgment.
4
Apply the refined definition
Using the author's final formulation: if each part cannot be replaced by a single term without losing the detailed judgment, the proposition is conditional. Otherwise, it is categorical.
This reconciles the earlier 'resolution into two propositions' criterion with the author's analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحكوم عليه وبه في القضيّة إن كانا مفردين سميت حملية وإلا فشرطية» هذا هو المطابق لماذكره الشيخ في الشفاء ) وقيل : صوابه أن يقال : «القضية إن انحلت إلى قضيتين فهي شرطية، وإلا فحمليّة لئلا يرد عليه مثل قولنا : «زيد أبوه قائم » فإنّه حمليّة مع أنه لم ينحل إلى مفردين، لأن المحكوم به فيه قضية. وهو ليس بصواب من وجهين : أما أولا فلورود بعض النقوض المذكورة عليه. وأما ثانياً فلأن انحلال القضية إلى مامنه تركيبها، أن كل حملية يمكن أن يعبر عن طرفيها - مع ملاحظة الارتباط - بمفردين، وأن الشرطية لا يمكن فيها ذلك (شريف). (1) راجع الشفاء : العبارة، ٣٢-٣٣، الفصل الخامس. (٢) وهو قولنا : زيد عالم يضاده زيد ليس بعالم وقولنا : « الشمس طالعة يلزمه النهار موجود (شریف) (٣) لأن المركب إنما ينحل إلى أجزائه الموجودة فيه لما عرفت من أن التحليل هو إبطال الصورة فلا يبقى إلا الأجزاء المادية، ثم إن أطراف الشرطية ليست قضايا، لأن القضية لاتتم إلا إذا اعتبر فيها الحكم إيقاعاً أو انتزاعاً، وما اعتبر فيه ذلك لا يرتبط بغيره ضرورة، فإنّك إذا قلت : «الشمس طالعة» وأوقعت النسبة بين طرفيه لم يتصور ربطه بشيء آخر بأن يصير محكوماً عليه أو به، فعالم تجرد القضية عن الحكم لم يمكن جعلها جزء قضية أخرى، فإذا حذفت أدوات الشرط والجزاء بقي «الشمس طالعة» و«النهار موجود بذلك المعنى الذي كان عليه حال الارتباط، فإنه بهذا المعنى كان موجوداً في الشرطية، فلا يكون قضية مالم يضم إليه الحكم، وحينئذ لا يكون ذلك تحليلاً فقط - بل تحليلاً إلى الأجزاء وضمّ شيء آخر إليها - ومن زعم أنه إذا حذفت الأدوات فقد وجد الحكم في الأطراف فقد أخطأ، وكيف يتوهم ذلك في مثل قولك : إن كان زيد حماراً كان ناهقاً مع العلم بكذب الطرفين وصدق الشرطية. لا يقال : «الأدوات كانت مانعة عن الحكم ، فإذا زالت عاد الحكم»، لأنّ زوال المانع لا يكفي في وجود الشيء، بل لابد من وجود المقتضي، وزوال المانع لا يستلزمه كما في المثال المذكور. وإن أردت تفصيلا يتضح به عليك الحال فاستمع لما نقول : القضية إن لم يوجد في شيء من طرفيها نسبة فهي حمليّة، كقولك : «الإنسان حيوان» وإن وجدت، فإن كانت مما لا يصح أن تكون تامه - بأن تكون نسبة تقييدية - فهي أيضاً حملية، كقولنا : الحيوان الناطق جسم ضاحك»، وإن كانت مما يصح أن تكون تامه، فإما أن توجد في أحد طرفيها، فتكون القضية أيضاً حملية، كقولك : «زيد أبوه قائم»، وإما أن توجد فيهما معاً، فإما أن تكون ملحوظة إجمالاً، فتكون أيضاً حملية، كقولك: «زيـد قـائـم ينافيـه زيـد لـيـس بقائم»، وإما أن تكون ملحوظة تفصيلاً، فتكون القضية شرطية، كقولنا : «إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود». فظهر أن أطراف الحملية إما مفردة بالفعل أو بالقوة، فإن المشتمل على النسبة التقييدية مطلقاً أو الخبرية إذا كانت ملحوظة إجمالا ممايمكن أن يوضع موضعه مفرد، لأنّ دلالته إجمالية ؛ وأن أطراف الشرطية لا يمكن وضع المفردات في مواضعها إذ لا يمكن أن يستفاد من المفردات ملاحظة المحكوم عليـه وبـه والنسبة الحكمية على التفصيل ؛ فإن شئت قلت في تقسيم القضية : «طرفاها إما أن يكونا مفردين بالفعل أو بالقوة أو لا، وإن شئت قلت : «كلّ واحد من طرفيها إما أن يكون مشتملاً على نسبة تامة ملحوظة تفصيلاً أو لا ». وكان من قال : «القضيّة إن انحلت إلى قضيتين » أراد أن كل واحد من طرفيها قضية بالقوة ملحوظة تفصيلاً، فيكون قضية بالقوة القريبة من الفعل، فيصح التقسيم بهذا الوجه أيضاً.
"The subject and predicate in the proposition, if they are both single terms, it is called categorical, otherwise conditional." This agrees with what the Shaykh mentioned in al-Shifa. And it is said: The correct thing is to say: "The proposition, if it resolves into two propositions, then it is conditional; otherwise it is categorical," lest there be an objection against it like our saying: "Zayd, his father is standing," for it is categorical even though it does not resolve into two single terms, because the predicate in it is a proposition. But this is not correct from two aspects: First, because some of the mentioned objections apply to it. Second, because the resolution of the proposition into what it is composed of [shows] that every categorical proposition can have its two extremes expressed—with attention to the connection—as two single terms, and that in the conditional proposition this is not possible (Sharif). (1) Refer to al-Shifa: al-‘Ibara, 32-33, chapter five. (2) That is our saying: "Zayd is knowledgeable opposes Zayd is not knowledgeable," and our saying: "The sun is rising entails daytime exists" (Sharif). (3) Because a composite only resolves into its parts existing in it, as you know that analysis is the removal of the form, so nothing remains but the material parts. Moreover, the parts of the conditional are not propositions, because a proposition is only complete when the judgment is considered in it as affirmation or negation, and what is considered in that way is necessarily not connected to something else. For if you say "The sun is rising" and you affirm the relation between its two terms, it is inconceivable to connect it to something else so that it becomes subject or predicate. Therefore, once the proposition is stripped of judgment, it cannot be made part of another proposition. So if you remove the tools of condition and consequence, there remains "the sun is rising" and "daytime exists" in that sense which they had in the state of connection. For in that sense they existed in the conditional, so they are not a proposition unless judgment is added to them. And then it is not merely analysis—but analysis into parts and adding something else to them. And whoever claims that when the tools are removed the judgment is found in the parts has erred. How could that be imagined in something like your saying: "If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray," while knowing the falsity of both parts and the truth of the conditional? It cannot be said: "The tools were preventing the judgment, so when the prevention is removed the judgment returns," because the removal of the preventer is not sufficient for the existence of a thing; rather, the existence of the efficient cause is necessary, and the removal of the preventer does not necessitate it, as in the mentioned example. And if you desire a detailed explanation that clarifies the matter for you, listen to what we say: The proposition, if a relation does not exist in any of its two parts, then it is categorical, like your saying: "Man is an animal." And if it exists, then if it is not such that it can be complete—i.e., it is a restrictive relation—then it is also categorical, like our saying: "Rational animal is a laughing body." And if it is such that it can be complete, then either it exists in one of its two parts, in which case the proposition is also categorical, like your saying: "Zayd, his father is standing." Or it exists in both of them together. In that case, either it is considered in summary, in which case it is also categorical, like your saying: "Zayd standing opposes Zayd not standing." Or it is considered in detail, in which case the proposition is conditional, like our saying: "If the sun is rising, then daytime exists." Thus it is clear that the parts of a categorical proposition are either single terms in actuality or in potentiality. For that which contains a restrictive relation absolutely or a predicative relation when considered in summary can have a single term put in its place, because its indication is summary. And the parts of the conditional cannot have single terms put in their places, because it is not possible from single terms to obtain the consideration of subject, predicate, and the judgmental relation in detail. For if you wish, you can say in the division of the proposition: "Its two parts are either single terms in actuality or potentiality or not." And if you wish, you can say: "Each of its two parts either does or does not contain a complete relation considered in detail." And [the one] who said: "The proposition, if it resolves into two propositions" meant that each of its two parts is a proposition in potentiality considered in detail, so it is a proposition in potentiality close to actuality. Thus the division is valid in this way as well."
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion and refinement of the division of propositions into categorical (حملية) and conditional (شرطية). The author begins by quoting a standard definition: a proposition is categorical if both the subject and predicate are single terms, otherwise conditional. He notes that this definition agrees with Avicenna’s statement in al-Shifa. However, a proposed correction is mentioned: instead, the criterion should be whether the proposition resolves into two propositions. This correction is intended to avoid objections like the example 'Zayd, his father is standing,' which is categorical but contains a proposition as its predicate. The author then refutes this correction from two angles. First, some objections still apply. Second, the notion of 'resolution' is problematic: a categorical proposition can always have its extremes expressed as single terms with the connection considered, while a conditional cannot. The author then elaborates with a detailed classification. He explains that the parts of a conditional are not actually propositions because the judgment is suspended by the conditional tools; when the tools are removed, what remains is not a proposition unless judgment is added. This is illustrated with the example 'If Zayd were a donkey, he would bray,' where both parts are false yet the conditional is true. The author then provides a systematic classification based on the presence and type of relation (نسبة) in the parts. The classification proceeds stepwise: if no relation exists in either part (e.g., 'Man is animal'), it is categorical. If a relation exists but is not complete (restrictive), also categorical (e.g., 'Rational animal is a laughing body'). If the relation can be complete, it may appear in one part (e.g., 'Zayd, his father is standing'), still categorical. If in both parts, it may be considered summarily (e.g., 'Zayd standing opposes Zayd not standing'), still categorical, or in detail, yielding a conditional (e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists'). The conclusion is that categorical parts are single terms actually or potentially, while conditional parts cannot be replaced by single terms because they require detailed consideration of subject, predicate, and judgment. Finally, the author reconciles the earlier proposed correction by noting that 'resolution into two propositions' means each part is a proposition in potentiality considered in detail, making the division valid on that understanding. Throughout, the author uses examples from logic (e.g., 'Man is animal', 'The sun is rising entails daytime') to illustrate the distinctions.

Key Terms
قضية
حمليّة
شرطية
نسبة تامة
تحليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a conditional proposition?
A) Man is an animal.
B) Zayd, his father is standing.
C) Zayd standing opposes Zayd not standing.
D) If the sun is rising, then daytime exists.

Key Takeaway
The refined criterion for a conditional proposition is that both its parts contain a complete relation considered in detail, making it impossible to replace those parts with single terms without losing the detailed judgment.

(b) 'If a woman menstruates, she does not pray' (from fiqh). (c) 'Zayd knowing opposes Zayd not knowing' (from logic). Explain your reasoning for each.

The Eight Conditions for Contradiction and Farabi's Reduction
Details eight conditions and Farabi's reduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the eight conditions for contradiction and explain how al-Fārābī reduces them to the unity of the judgmental relation.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires unity in eight aspects, but al-Fārābī shows one condition suffices: the judgmental relation.

In this passage, the author details the eight conditions (al-umūr al-thamāniyya) that must be met for two propositions to be truly contradictory. He then presents al-Fārābī's insight that all eight are subsumed under a single condition: the unity of the judgmental relation (nisba ḥukmiyya). Understanding this reduction is essential for analysing contradictions in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight conditions
List the conditions: unity of subject, predicate, condition, whole/part, time, place, relation, potentiality, and actuality.
The first two are explicit; others are subsumed under subject or predicate.
2
See how condition and whole/part are subsumed under subject
The author shows that a difference in condition (white vs. black body) or whole vs. part (Zanjī) changes the subject, so subject unity is conceptual, not just linguistic.
3
See how time, place, etc. are subsumed under predicate
Example: 'Zayd is sleeping' at night vs. 'Zayd is not sleeping' by day – the predicate differs because the temporal condition differs.
Similarly for place, relation, etc., by analogy.
4
Understand al-Fārābī's reduction
He reduces all to unity of the judgmental relation: if the relation between subject and predicate is identical, all eight conditions are unified. Any difference in the eight changes the relation.
This is a powerful simplification.
5
Apply to an example
Consider 'Zayd is generous' and 'Zayd is not generous'. They are contradictory only if the subject, predicate, condition, etc. are identical. If one refers to generosity with wealth and the other with time, they are not contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما اندراج وحدة الشرط : فلأن الموضوع في قولنا : «الجسم مفرّق للبصر هو الجسم لا مطلقاً - بل بشرط كونه أبيض – والموضوع في قولنا : «الجسم ليس بمفرق للبصر » هو الجسم لا مطلقاً، بل بشرط كونه أسود - فاختلاف الشرط يستتبع اختلاف الموضوع، فلواتحد الموضوع اتحد الشرط. وإما اندراج وحدة الكل والجزء : فلأن الموضوع في قولنا : «الزنجي أسود» بعض الزنجي وفي قولنا : الزنجي ليس بأسود كل الزنجي، وهما مختلفان.
ووحدة المحمول يندرج فيها الوحدات الباقية :
أما اندراج وحدة الزمان فلأن المحمول في قولنا : «زيد نائم» النائم ليلاً، وفي قولنا زيد ليس بنائم النائم نهاراً، فاختلاف الزمان يستدعي اختلاف المحمول.
وإما اندراج وحدة المكان والإضافة والقوة والفعل فعلى ذلك القياس.
وردّها الفارابي إلى وحدة واحدة وهي وحدة النسبة الحكمية حتى يكون السلب وارداً على النسبة التي ورد عليها الإيجاب، وعند ذلك يتحقق التناقض جزماً، وانما كانت مردودة إلى تلك الوحدة، لأنه إذا اختلف شيء من الأمور الثمانية اختلفت النسبة، ضرورة أن نسبة المحمول إلى أحد الأمرين مغايرة لنسبته إلى الآخر، ونسبة أحد الأمرين إلى شيء مغايرة لنسبة الآخر إليه، ونسبة أحد الأمرين إلى الآخر بشرط مغايرة للنسبة إليه بشرط آخر، وعلى هذا فمتى اتحدت النسبة اتحد الكل. وإن كان القضيتان محصورتين فلابد مع ذلك – أي مع اتحادهما في الأمور الثمانية – من اختلافهما في الكم، أي في الكلية والجزئية، فإنهما لو كانتا كليتين أو جزئيتين لم يتناقضا لجواز كذب الكليتين وصدق الجزئيتين في كل مادة يكون الموضوع فيها أعم من المحمول، كقولنا : كل حيوان إنسان» و «لاشيء من الحيوان بإنسان» فإنهما كاذبتان، وكقولنا : بعض الحيوان إنسان» و «بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان» فإنهما صادقتان.
As for the inclusion of the unity of condition: because the subject in our statement 'The body is a disperser of sight' is the body not absolutely but conditioned by being white, and the subject in our statement 'The body is not a disperser of sight' is the body not absolutely but conditioned by being black; so difference of condition entails difference of subject, for if the subject were one, the condition would be one. As for the inclusion of the unity of whole and part: because the subject in our statement 'The Zanjī is black' is part of the Zanjī, and in our statement 'The Zanjī is not black' is the whole Zanjī, and they are different. And the unity of predicate subsumes the remaining unities: as for the inclusion of the unity of time, because the predicate in our statement 'Zayd is sleeping' is the one who sleeps at night, and in our statement 'Zayd is not sleeping' is the one who sleeps by day; so difference of time entails difference of predicate. And as for the inclusion of the unity of place, relation, potentiality and actuality, it is by analogy. And al-Fārābī reduced them to a single unity, namely the unity of the judgmental relation, so that the negation applies to the same relation as the affirmation, and thereby contradiction is definitely realized. And they were reduced to that unity because if any of the eight matters differ, the relation differs, necessarily because the relation of the predicate to one of the two matters is different from its relation to the other, and the relation of one of the two matters to a thing is different from the relation of the other to it, and the relation of one of the two matters to the other under a condition is different from the relation to it under another condition. Thus, whenever the relation is unified, everything is unified. And if the two propositions are quantified, then along with that—i.e., along with their unity in the eight matters—there must be a difference in quantity, i.e., in universality and particularity; for if they were both universal or both particular, they would not be contradictory because of the possibility of both universals being false and both particulars being true in any matter where the subject is more general than the predicate, like our statement 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human'—both are false—and like our statement 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human'—both are true.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage explains the eight conditions for contradiction (tanāquḍ) between two propositions, showing how various unities are subsumed under the unity of subject and predicate. The author begins with the unity of condition: for example, in 'The body is a disperser of sight' and 'The body is not a disperser of sight', the subject is the same body but under different conditions (white vs. black), so the condition must be the same for true contradiction. Next, the unity of whole and part is discussed using the example of 'The Zanjī is black' and 'The Zanjī is not black': in the affirmative, 'the Zanjī' refers to a part, while in the negative it refers to the whole, so the subjects differ. Then the unity of predicate is shown to subsume the remaining unities (time, place, relation, potentiality, actuality) by analogy: for instance, 'Zayd is sleeping' at night vs. 'Zayd is not sleeping' by day—the predicate changes because the temporal condition differs. After this, al-Fārābī is cited as reducing all eight conditions to the single unity of the judgmental relation (al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya). The reasoning is that any difference in the eight matters results in a difference in the relation between subject and predicate, so if the relation is the same, all conditions are unified. The passage then notes that for quantified propositions, there is an additional requirement: the two propositions must differ in quantity (universal vs. particular). Without this difference, both universals could be false (e.g., 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human') and both particulars could be true (e.g., 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human'), so contradiction would not occur.

تناقض
وحدة
نسبة حكمية

Checkpoint
In the example 'The body is a disperser of sight' and 'The body is not a disperser of sight', if the first refers to a white body and the second to a black body, why are they not contradictory?
A) Because the predicates differ.
B) Because the subject is different due to the condition.
C) Because the quantity differs.
D) Because the time differs.

Key Takeaway
The eight conditions for contradiction are reducible to the unity of the judgmental relation, a point stressed by al-Fārābī.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct two propositions about the attribute of divine will (irāda) that appear contradictory but are not due to a difference in one of the eight conditions. Use a kalam example.

8 min
The Additional Condition for Quantified Propositions
Adds quantity condition for quantified propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two quantified propositions are contradictory and explain why they must differ in quantity.

Core Idea
Contradiction between universals or between particulars is impossible; one must be universal and the other particular.

After establishing the eight conditions for contradiction, the author adds a crucial point for quantified propositions: they must differ in quantity (universal vs. particular). Without this, both universals can be false and both particulars can be true, as shown by examples from animal/human. This ensures that contradictory propositions cannot both be false or both be true.

Guided Path
1
Recall the eight conditions
For quantified propositions, the eight conditions must be met, plus an additional condition.
This builds on the previous lesson.
2
Understand why two universals cannot be contradictory
Example: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human' are both false. Therefore they are not contradictory because both are false.
Contradictories must have opposite truth values.
3
Understand why two particulars cannot be contradictory
Example: 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is not a human' are both true. Therefore they are not contradictory.
They are subcontraries.
4
Apply the condition
For contradictions in quantified propositions, one must be universal and the other particular (e.g., 'Every animal is a human' vs. 'Some animal is not a human' are contradictories).
This is the square of opposition.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Take 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Some intoxicant is not forbidden'. These are contradictories if the eight conditions hold.
This shows the practical use in fiqh.
